RATING: 8
Resource management, placement game with very limited actions, and some elements of randomness.

This is a decent, although somewhat light game, where on your turn you acquire resources and/or craftsmen, followed by 3 turns of placing your builders to acquire points, money, more resources, etc., and then finally at the end of the round you convert your resources into points via the use of your craftsmen.There are a few random elements such as the event cards each round (half good, half bad), the order of the builders that come out, as well as the resource, craftsmen, and special cards that are available for purchase or placement each round.My wife actually quite likes this game, I was initially a bit disappointed with it, but after several plays, I've upped my rating/opinion of it- it's accessible, yet has a decent amount of strategy, and it's short playing time (6 rounds) allows it to make it to the table more frequently.  The expansion really opens up things and adds to the replayability (and will make playing 4-players alot more manageable).  My biggest gripe now is that all of the cards (and there are alot of them, especially with the expansion) are those stupid tiny hobbit sized ones- give us some regular-sized cards for goodness sake!!!

Own the [boardgame=31753]expansion[/boardgame] set which certainly adds variablility to the game through a new board (new placements), and new cards (event and characters), and adds the ability to play up to 6 players (not sure if this is good or not).

RATING: 5.5
I appreciate the aesthetics of this one - it does look very nice indeed. But it's just an extreme worker placement game with 14 choices. The randomness of drawing out of the bag didn't bother me. It was instead the labor of unrolling all the placement areas that dried up the game. And I felt there were certain paths to victory, certain card combinations that were superior - the experienced player would know it and crush the newbies. Not a good thing in a game at all because it disenfranchises the new player and makes certain a short life span for the game - once you "get it", you're done.

RATING: 7.5
Simple, elegant and versatile with a nice amount of choices to establish several paths to victory. Beautiful to look at, thanks to Artist Michael Menzel, and very nice component quality .  It is simple enough for non-frequent gamers or new comers to understand, and complicated enough for gamers to enjoy.  Based on the bestselling book, this game has a similar theme to Caylus, you are a Master Builder, gathering resources in order to build the cathedral and get victory points....However this game does not have the level of complexity that Caylus offers.

RATING: N/A
Scoperta: 2 punti.
Vittoria: 8 punti.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
I need more plays before I can make a more informed assessment of this game.  A lot of times I have a good idea how I feel about a game after one play, but that's not the case here.  There are certainly a few elements of this game that immediately jumped out at me (in a good way).  

I really like the mechanic associated with assigning the master builders.  Do you pay the cost to assign your builder right away when he is drawn from the bag to ensure you get the action you want, or do you wait until later to assign him so that doing so is free?  Money is tight, so that always makes for a tough decision.

The game is relatively simple to learn, and I think it makes for a good "gateway" game.  It didn't take us long to figure out at all, although this might reflect our experience with similar worker-placement games.

Speaking of which, this certainly bears similarities to other games out there, such as Stone Age and Caylus.  The one downside to Pillars is that I think I'd rather play these other similar games (despite liking Pillars), so I'm not sure how much it will get played.

My last observation for the moment is that the game does feel subtly monotonous to me and it might drag on for one round more than it should.  Every round feels very similar, and by the sixth round I'm ready for the game to be over.  I think 4-5 rounds would have hit the sweet spot for me in terms of game length.  However, I'm sure 6 rounds is needed for balancing purposes and to incorporate the ever-improving craftsman that are only revealed in the late rounds (I also like that element of the game--i.e. the changing quality of the craftsmen available).

UPDATE (12-06-2014): Just played a 3-player game and we all really enjoyed it.  All of us rated it around an 8.5.

RATING: 8
First time playing Pillars...I think this is a fun worker placement game with lots of interesting decisions.

Admittedly, I had mixed feelings about the randomness introduced into the game by "events cards," and "the draw from the bag" mechanic of the second round of worker placements in each turn.

But...was it my imagination, or was the game so well designed and play-tested that it all kind of balanced out such that you got the fun and excitement of surprises, but these surprises did not decisively determine the outcome of the game? I thought that this was the case, but I guess I would have to play Pillars again to get a better idea.

But the bottom line was that many of the mechanics, choices, and surprises seemed fun and challenging to think about...at least for fans of worker placement games like me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
"The patriarch of worker placement games" as Sam Healey put it. And he's right. Pillars of the Earth is easily one of the best produced and best designed Euro games you will find. Plus it has the innovation of drawing workers randomly from a bag to see when or if you'll place your worker first or later. Wanna place your worker first? It'll cost you. The sooner you place the more you pay but you get access to what you need sooner. Place later and it won't cost nearly as much but you may get shut out of what you want and have to settle for the left overs. The decision is yours. And as the game goes along you get characters with special abilities to help you out in future rounds. But only if you can get them soon enough. Thus paying more. Pillars plays smooth and clean without any hard to follow rules or fiddly mechanisms. This one is a must own.

RATING: 7.5
This is a solid well designed game.  The pace and duration are spot on and feel very natural.  The random elements can leave you wondering what you did wrong if things don't fall your way and this is likely the biggest problem with the design.  If you can manage your money well, it should be possible to offset a bad turn of master builder draws.  Several bad turns of draws are very difficult to overcome.  Turn order can be critical, but knowing when it is important and when it is not is not easy to predict.  The game design allows for the players to control this, so there is a mechanism to offset turn order advantages.  Overall, the game gives you the feeling that you can always try another approach and it is this that ultimately keeps you coming back.

RATING: 4
It's an ok game. However, it's hard to classify it. It's very light if you're seeking a deep strategy worker-placement game (try Caylus instead). It's dry and heavy if you want a fast, fun, worker-placement game (try Stone Age instead). It's too simple for a deep economic engine game (try Puerto Rico or a myriad others instead), and too complex for a casual resource management game (try Blue Moon City instead).

It's a game you simply can't seem to find "the perfect time" to play it. And for a game as expensive as this one (including a worthless expensive component, and not much discount from online retailers), this is a definite no-no.

I'd play it once in a while. I would certainly not but it. Ever.

RATING: 8
Out of Print (OOP).

I enjoyed how the game had 2 parts (1. selection of resources gathering, craftsman for hire as well as crusades and 2. placing of master builders) and yet they work beautifully together.

Furthermore the fact that the game is well balanced where no one gets to always place their master builder first PLUS the last master builder is ordered in a such a way that the first is last and the last is first allowed the game to be more enjoyable to all as a while.

Few rules which I always forget it that you can only have 5 resources to carry over each round and each time you fail to have enough cash to make any payment, you would subtract 1 victory for every shortfall of 2 gold.

The money track is also ingenious. I love this idea, rather than dishing out more paper currency.

RATING: 4
Played once with 6 players using the expansion stuff.  I knew going in that this was going to be a very random worker placement game.  However, I had no idea that this game featured players randomly taking turns during the Master Builder phase.  In a six player game it's highly possible to be starting player and end up getting the 11th and 12th pick!  Other than being the most random worker placement game ever, I was happy and impressed with the bits.  

FYI- Pillars felt more card drafting than worker placement!  Other than acquiring resources, this game has nothing to do with Caylus.  I consider Pillars as a distant nephew of Caylus and a closer relative to either Saint Petersburg, Tribune, Leonardo da Vinci, or Age of Empires III.

RATING: 9
This is my favourite "traditional board game" that I've played so far.
I love the breadth and depth of choices each player makes during all steps of the game. There is almost no down time for players as everybody is interested in the action at all times. Every choice is a difficult one with so many options of where to play your master builders or what to buy with your workers. 
All this combined with a lovely theme from an entertaining books makes for a masterful game.
I highly recommend the expansion which fixes some issues with worker placement and makes the game fun with all the way up to 6 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This indeed does have the kind of place workers mechanic that I like about Caylus and AOEIII. It's the one where if you do something first, you are denying somebody else. Delicious.

[b]Summary[/b]
Basically you are converting three different resources into VPs. I guess the VPs represent your contributions to the Cathedral. There are 6 turns. Each turn you draw worker allocations and some craftsmen to choose from. You can spend money to get a craftsman or you can wait and claim one with one of your Master Builders. As you claim worker allocations you place your workers into one of the three resource pits.

Your workers will generate the resources that you will convert into VPs through your craftsmen. After workers are placed. The starting player reaches into the bag and begins drawing Master Builder pawns. The Master Builders don't "do" anything but claim spots on the board that will allow you improve your situation in various ways like getting a special ability, gaining immunity from bad events (insurance?), gain VPs, gain metal from the King, visit the court to avoid being taxed, acquire a craftsman, gain two workers for the next round, visit the market or become the start player.

After you've claimed these actions with your Master Builders, you resolve them in order. After resolving actions, you then decide how many resources you want to convert to VPs. It's good to save some for the next round because you'll get a chance at improving your efficiency at turning resources into VPs at a better rate. You can only save, however, up to 5 resources from one round to the next.

RATING: 6
A very good game that I really enjoy playing.  I will give a couple of problems, though, since it's unfortunately easier to talk about  bad things than good things.

First, it is a pretty long game, but mercifully it does not feel as long as some other long games.

Second, it is very annoying in a 4-player game if 3 of the last 4 master builders in the bag are your own, since there really aren't that many useful positions on the board.  I think 3 players would probably be the sweet spot for this game.

Third and this is the big one for me, it feels like new players will get completely schooled by experienced players.  The same four craftsmen will always appear in each round, and knowing what will come up later is a huge advantage.  The only strategy you can have is based on information that another player could not possibly know, and that doesn't sit well with me.

Not dealbreakers though.  The second problem is only a rare problem and only with 4 players, the third is a bigger problem for me, but both [i]should[/i] be alleviated greatly with the expansion, with an extra board and extra cards to mix in with the others.  Playing with 5 or 6 players would make the first problem worse... but I'm not worried about that at this point in time.

I like it, and will happily play it pretty much anytime.  Now to see if I can give the expansion a try.

RATING: 8
This game is an excellent worker placement game.  It has two types of workers (meeples and master builders).  The payment scheme and random selection and pass capabilities of the master builders is awesome.  I really like that system.  However, the game has less depth than Agricola or Caylus, and in fact the game penalizes long term planning with resource hoarding limits.  It seems to boil down to grabbing the best craftsman and privilege cards by being lucky enough to be first when they are drawn.  There is definitely tactical play in the game, but I didn't feel I was working toward something "bigger" like I do when I play Agricola and Caylus.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Another worker placement game, this one is based on the Ken Follett book of the same name. Thematically one is contributing to the building of the great cathedral at Kingsbridge, but in fact you are collecting resources which then are turned in for VPs through a process of assigning workers to collect building materials (sand,  stone and wood) and then turning what you've collected into vps via craftsmen cards of increasing efficiency as the game goes along. The actual construction of the cathedral acted solely as a clock as a new piece is placed at the end of each turn (six pieces= a six turn game).

Turn order is initially random with the cost of placing your three "master builders" early (on spots which garner craftsmen or other benefit cards) being higher than if you are going later - but by going early in the placement order you are usually getting a better choice of the resource options (worker placement) and the craftsmen cards (master builders) and/or other benefits. Money was very tight in the one game I played so often you couldn't afford to go early with your builders and had to hope something decent would be left later, but you still wanted to have the better resource choices - the bottom line was turn order really matters...

About that one play. You absolutely must play this game a couple of times to avoid real bad mistakes because you don't know the pacing of the craftsmen card progression. You run the risk of paying a lot of scarce money for cards that prove not to be worth their cost (and probably explains why money was so tight for us) and high early costs are hard to recoup.

Nothing really bad to say and I would play again, but I'm not likely to purchase this based on what I've seen so far.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Totally overproduced - feels a bit like Caylus, and of course the theme is similar (build something). However this game introduces lots of randomness that you don't have in caylus. Events, might be good, might not, etc. Biggest downside for me is you can't really plan a strategy as you have little control over your selection, as the master builders are drawn from the sack. All in all the randomness overwhelms any strategy and instead you play a tactical game trying to maximise the value of each action in a turn. Caylus works for me because you can set on a strategy (collect cubes, build residence, collect cash etc), here that element is gone. Similar to Caylus in a way RailRoad Tycoon is similar to Age of Steam - and like that comparison I prefer the original. In this case I don't like the original that much either!

** Update, played again, opinion confirmed, definitely don't like this much at all - if I want to play a worker placement game with limited resources to buy stuff to get you vps type euro game I'll play Caylus over this everytime. Rating dropped a point, doubt I'll ever play this again.

RATING: 8
juego de mecánica similar al StoneAge. Lo principal es el Workmen placement. Interesante sustitución de dinero por marcador. RATING: 7.5
Juego de colocación de trabajadores con un sistema original de elección de inicativa. Los trabajadores de los jugadores se van sacando de una bolsa y el propietario decide si colocar el trabajador asumiendo un coste, o pasar y colocarlo gratuitamente cuando se hayan sacado todos los trabajadores de la bolsa.
Está ambientado en la novela de ken follet, y va sobre la construcción de una catedral. La partida dura seis turnos y está bastante entretenido. Es sencillo y familiar.
Dura un poco más de una hora.

El juego pertenece estrictamente a mi hermano, pero tras tenerlo en casa muerto de risa durante los últimos cuatro años, decidí llevarmelo e incorporarlo a mi colección, en Enero de 2015.

Cambiado, junto al la expansion por un La Granja a illumuli de la BSK, el 26/08/2015 (+ 5 euros)

RATING: 7.5
I've never read Ken Follett's novel, but I quite like the game. It's good to see a game where you don't have to be intimately interested in the subject matter to have a good time.
The game is all about competing with other players for optimal placement of your pieces to maximize your resource generation to help you become the most influential builder of a Medieval cathedral. I find that with two players, the competition is reduced to a point where the game can get a little dull: you don't really need to worry about missing out on the resources you need because with only one competitor they will likely not go anywhere before they get to you. So, I would definitely recommend three or four players on this one.

RATING: 8
Based loosely on the book insofar as it is about building a cathedral in the 13th century. It is a worker placement game with a board similar to stone age. Instead of dice you place a set number of workers in each area to gain a set amount of resources. Once all your plebs are out you have master builders who you can use to make more moves on the board. I love worker placements and although it is similar to Stone Age it gives a bit more depth without too much luck (no dice rolling for resources). A great game to have in any collection.

RATING: 6

===========
Caylus lite.  I like it, but the random factors can really screw you.  When 2 of the 3 resource production cards for a given resource are out of play, anyone relying on that resource gets majorly screwed.  Having a balanced VP production engine helps you to avoid that problem, but you will lose every time to the player who optimizes on one resource and gets lucky with that resource always being available.  I would rather play Caylus.

RATING: 7
I like this a lot. In our first game, we basically played randomly for the first few rounds. But by halfway through, I finally understood what you should be trying to do in each round (basically, gather the building materials for your craftsmen). My ability to plan for the longer term is still nonexistent, but oh well.

I don't have a problem with the random draw of the master builders (in fact, I think that mechanic is quite ingenious), but I [i]do[/i] have a problem with the random events. Aside from placing a master builder in the bishop's seat, how do you prepare for those events? Does your planning have to account for the chance that ANY of them could come up? That seems absurd. And yet a single event could scuttle your careful allocation of resources for the round. In fact, the event for the last round in our first game deprived my wife of about 10 points (though she won anyway).

RATING: 8
As I've played Pillars some more, I've become more comfortable with the game and more comfortable with my 8 rating. I might even kick it up to a 9 at some point for the combined basic/expansion 4-player game; I think the expansion really helps in giving a little more variety to the flow of craftspeople, which I think is a legitimate complaint about the "basic" game.

Another win for Pillars is that as I've played, I've become more attracted to the theme. The "building a castle/church/cathedral" has become such  a staple of unimaginative game themes, it was easy to instinctively put Pillars in that category. But I think it's actually much better than that. As you start with low-VP masons laying the foundations and progress to high-VP sculptors and bell-makers, the game has a nice, evocative arc. The gorgeous presentation helps a lot too.
--
I've played Pillars of the Earth a few more times recently, and it's been growing on me. Part of this may be the fact that it's simply better and more interesting than the raft of mediocre-to-awful worker-placement type games we've seen of late (Kingsburg comes immediately to mind), and so by comparison it looks good. No question it's a tight, well-executed game, which most games these days are not. But there is a part of me that won't let me rate it an 8 because of a sneaking suspicion that a lot of the enjoyment for me is that I literally win this game all the time. The only time I lost was the very first game in which we played the rules wrong. I kid you not, in the last half-dozen games I have played, I have not lost and it has rarely been close. Fun for me, but I'm fairly sure I win just because I've played more - I'm not convinced there is much of a range of viable strategies.

OK, what the hell, I'll give it an 8.
---
http://homepage.mac.com/c_farrell/iblog/C2097221587/E20061221220133/index.html
---
I've since played some more, and come to like the game a little better. Not enough to kick it up to an 8, but it's fun, and right on the bubble for me between a "buy/not-a-buy", so since I'm trying to buy fewer games, I'll try not buying it, see how that works out, unless I can find one in a fire sale somewhere. I think the luck of the draw in the master builders and the events is just a bit too heavy, and the game is a bit too scripted with the prescribed progression of craftspeople. It's still a decent game, but there are similar, related games (like Aladdin's Dragons) that are just more fun. Given the choice between this and Caylus, though, I'll take this any day.
---
UPDATE: Pillars has now almost (but not quite) reached nickel status, and I have to say I'm glad to play it from time to time but also glad I didn't buy it.  The game just lacks real tension and variability. It's well put-together and plays well, but it just doesn't have any edge. Compared to the recent Phoenicia, Phoenicia is a far more compelling game that does more with less. Like Phoenicia, though, one thing one has to give Pillars of the Earth credit for is that it's a basically economic game that doesn't have any sort of runaway leader/compound interest problem. Anyway, nice, but I like my games a little edgier.

RATING: 5
I like games like this where you're slowly ramping up your output throughout the game.  I kind of like the master builder phase of the game, where you're never sure what order the master builders will come out, and usually have a tough decision to make as to where to put them.  However, while this mechanic provides for some tense moments, it can be frustrating at the same time as the cards you're making your points engine out of are fairly scarce, so you can really get boned out of a card you needed simply because your builder didn't get drawn.  Overall, I like this game alright but there are other games in the genre I'd rather play.

RATING: 5
[22/12/06] 'Die Säulen der Erde' bears more than a passing resemblance to Caylus and is often described as 'Caylus light' for good reason.I feel the game lacks balance since there are certain craftsmen who vastly superior. This railroads the game to the such a point that one begins to question whether the game's driving the action rather than the player himself. The tension and uncertainty witnessed in Caylus regarding your allocation of resources isn't quite there in this game, particularly as the disproportionate element of luck and lacking board development hamper this.Cathedral theme is a letdown. I regret the Building the cathedral itself is a mere VP track and the various cathedral pieces merely serve as a turn marker. The remarkable artwork rather than the mechanics is the main theme vehicle.Gameplay is IMO too ponderous, unoriginal, calculational and lacking sufficient direct interaction for it to be really a game I'd choose to play with family or casual gamers. With gaming ethusiasts on the other hand, I cannot see how I'd suggest Säulen der Erde over Caylus.I hope this isn't given the SdJ prize. That'd be a disservice to German families and casual gamers. Fortunately, I believe the jury will realise this.

RATING: 6.5
This was one of the first worker placement games I tried and I liked it a lot. I discovered the awesome world of WP games/ressource collecting->transforming into VP.
Since PotE I have played other better WP games which has caused me to downgrade PotE a bit, but it's still a good game. I like the luck aspect in it when drawing your master builders (though, admittedly, you can get screwed) and that different ressource cards appear every round so you cannot know for sure which ressources will be available. The artwork is gorgeous which definitely helps when introducing it to new players. 

However, there are some drawbacks: it seems like some of the craftsmen are better than others; the 1 wood for 4 gold is overpowered and if you get that card it seems like you have a distinct advantage in the game. Also, my games have been won with the metal craftsmen which seem quite strong, though the sculptors (stone) are great too. It's not impossible to win without having any metal, but in my experience it's going to be tough!

Furthermore, PotE isn't really for 2 players as there is not much tension as there are always decent spots to place your master builders on, but with 4 (and probably 3) it's a very classic light worker placement game in which you get ressource cubes and transform them into victory points.

RATING: 6
I had traded for Pillars because I was looking for an approachable worker placement game. The other options I had (Dominant Species, Age of Empires III, etc.) were too heavy for lighter gamers.

After one play, I'm fairly impressed. The game is easy to pick up, the rounds progress smoothly, everything moves fluidly. This is leaps and bounds better than something like Stone Age.

At the same time, I personally find it enjoyable but nothing earth shattering. Whether or not this stays in my collection will largely depend on if I find an audience of more casual gamers. 

Update: a second play, and I expect I will trade this away. It's an OK game, but it is stuck in some purgatory between too complex for "gateway worker placement game," but not interesting enough for my fellow gamers. I'd have to lobby hard to get this to the table, and I'm not interested enough in the game to do that.

Update: ...and yes, I successfully traded this away for Pandemic: Legacy.

RATING: 8
A very nice family strategy game. Some mechanisms reminded me of Caylus, others of Power Grid, but The Pillars of the Earth is less complex and more forgiving (it's a family game, after all).

I don't mind the randomness of the cards but the randomness of how the master builders get into play each round is a bit too much for me, and I would have preferred a turn order mechanic similar to the one used in Caylus.

What a shame that the building of the cathedral merely serves as a round marker rather than being part of the game play mechanics as it is the case in Elasund for example.

Absolutely gorgeous artwork. Should you ever burn out on it (and this may well happen if you play it too often, as it has a much lower replay value than, say, Caylus or Power Grid), just hang the board on the wall.

I rate it an 8 if played with the expansion, but just a 7 without it. I wouldn't want to play a 4-player game without the extra board any more, and the extra cards do add some variety.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Wow, this one was a bitter disappointment. I've read the novel and thought that the worker placement mechanic would be enjoyable. 

It COULD work, but I found myself really distracted by the turn-order bag draw. I understand that this supposedly is balanced by the decreasing cost of the actions, but I don't think it sufficiently balances anything. It feels like too much of the game is interrupted by this chaos, which detracts from the game-play. This in turn makes the game much more tactical than strategic, although an intimate knowledge of the cards does allow for some strategic planning....but whether the cards come available as actions or for purchase can dramatically change things.

You're forced to have so much flexibility that luck ends up being injected far too much.  Ultimately, if you get shafted on the draw bag, there is no mechanism to compensate and keep you in the game. I don't mind luck in games, but this design is at odds with that type of luck issue.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Säulen...this was not a game that I was in any particular hurry to play. Early reports came back that it was a Caylus variant with more randomness, and after having played it, I will confirm that analysis.

This is another game in the "you have limited resources with which to get other resources which can then be turned into victory points". Worker pieces are placed to get raw materials, and then your 3 special, larger workers can net you other privileges like tax evasion and better position in the turn order. The value of the resource cubes is driven by personality cards that you can draft and or purchase during the course of the game which essentially improve your victory point conversion rate. So an early personality might be able to get you 1 VP for 2 a given type of cube, a later one might be able to give you 1 VP for 1 of a given cube, and so forth. This is one part of the game where it felt more like Louis XIV rip off than a Caylus one (i.e.: the special power cards that players accrue), but again, I think the original game did it much better. There is also a money economy grafted onto the system, and that is combined with a strange random order selection, where player pieces are randomly drawn from a sack, and then that person has the right to either pay the current rate (which starts out very high) or to delay that worker's action until later in the round. This is a game like Caylus in that trying to explain it in a clean way is nigh impossible.

So I still like Caylus, and I found this to be almost completely derivative, with the changes all being for the worse. There is a significant random aspect in the timing of the personality cards arrival, in the "events" deck, and in the order the pieces are drawn out of the cloth bag. The bottom line then is this: if this was a new game, and Caylus didn't exist, it would probably rate decently because the system works in spite of all the shortcomings, but because Caylus does exist, I see absolutely zero reason to ever pull this out...it's simply a shorter, more random version of the same basic concept. Frankly, I find the lack of innovation here mildly offensive...scratch that, quite offensive. Given that I don't even think Caylus does the system best at this point (...Agricola) this is getting pretty far down the list of "action drafting" games for me...probably my least favourite honestly. I have no intention of ever playing this again.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
This is a beautiful game. I am impressed by how well the theme of the book related elements are tied into the game appropriately. I liked the components and the board and cards have excellent artwork throughout. Also being in the middle of reading the book for the first time added a lot to the feel of it. The characters were more than just names and faces on the cards.

The game left me feeling a little bit undecided as to whether it will become a favourite or just be a very good game. The first game confusion of not knowing the relative values of things or the pace and acceleration of the victory point production made it hard for me to assess how well it worked as a game for strategising. I thought I had gone for VPs too early with Prior Phillip and many direct points from his specialty area at the expense of getting craftsmen who were efficient. As it turned out I won after some fortunate finishing moves - getting the bellmaker and both final round special cards with free metal for all players from the previous rounds event card. This meant that though I lead throughout the whole game the feeling that I was going to be hauled in and come last did not eventuate and I won with a little space to spare. It helped (me anyway) that OzGamer who came second had a particularly disasterous last round as I kept taking all the things he wanted. Without the very lucky / very unlucky last round I thnk I would have been a contender for last place. 
This might become a favourite but it did seem a little too luck based and chaotic for strong planning and strategy. Not exceedingly so but enough to think that smart play can often be eroded by bad luck.

Second game. Again I was surprised to win and don't feel like I played well.

RATING: 8
Got this game back on the table after about 5 years and I have to say: It is still a good one. Usually I try to stay away from games that carry a franchise title because in my experience, 19 out of 20 of those games are not worth the cardboard they are printed on. The Pillars of the Earth is one of the few exceptions. Rules and components are well done as you would expect from a KOSMOS game and the game's worker placement mechanics work perfectly despite the strong theme they were built around (actually I don't know if this was the case or if theme and mechanism just happened to fit). In fact, the theme doesn't even bother me and this is probably the only game I like despite it's theme: I tried to read the novel (twice) after I played the game but I disliked it's triviality so much that I never read more than half of it before it got too boring to continue. But neither not knowing anything at all about the game's background theme nor disliking it has ever prevented the game from appealing to me. Of course there are recognizable persons and places but if you don't know them they are as good as any other random names. I have often used this game as an entry game for gamers who never played any complex games before but wanted to experience something more than Monopoly, Memory and Rummy had to offer - The Pillars of the Earth does a great job at that. (This is for the German KOSMOS (2006) edition.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
The first thing everyone noticed was the pretty board. Some similarities to Caylus were mentioned (which was also being played 2 player at the same time between Oren and Wade), and I guess I see that. It also felt a little like the more recent Leonardo Di Vinci in that you have workers, and you choose where to allocate them, which nets you resources. You then use workers (recipes) to turn resources into points. Interestingly, the board serves very little purpose in the game. It just has places to put stuff without much special significance. I never did quite figure out the ideal dollar/points ratio. It seems like knowing this would be very useful. I only had one minor nit with the game (still a good game as is), and would be interested in trying one of two slight modifications just for comparison. Variant 1: when someone pays to place, do not leave a gap; instead draw another pawn at the same number. Variant 2: when drawing pawns, pull two pawns per space. This means that no one gets to place for free until all pawns have been drawn. Variant 2.1: like variant 2, but draw both pawns for a number, and before the first one makes any decision, the second one can make an offer of money and/or cubes to go first. The player with the first pawn can accept it and be 2nd at the number, or reject it and still be 1st. Variant 1 is simplest, and would certainly go a long away to assuaging my bemoaning of someone else getting to place all three of their pawns for free before I got to place any of mine for free, while variant 2.1 would address the end-game issue we had where two players both desperately wanted to be picked first (willing to pay any price) just to be able to pick the card we both needed with no way to influence our order.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Components: Gorgeous board. The text on the board could be clearer, or at least have the corresponding numbers be larger. The cubes are great, as are the master builders and workers. The exclamation mark on the starting craftsmen is insufficient to indicate their added bonuses, which we overlooked and assumed were just rules of the game.

Experience: Hmm. Seems like a lot of pushing cubes around. Let's work backward. I'm trying to get points, and the main way to get points is to get cubes, and the main way to get cubes is to send workers to gather materials. The rest is somewhat interesting window dressing, to upgrade to better craftsmen; to get more money to be able to afford to upgrade to better craftsmen; to get special privileges; etc. I think there might be a little too much going on. Although I enjoy Agricola, I'm definitely more in my comfort zone with the relatively transparent operations of Stone Age. Pillars of the Earth feels pretty fiddley to me.

With only two players, it didn't seem to matter much whether we specialized. Specializing in a particular resource just means my opponent no longer has competition for the other resources.

I like that the privileges and events available each game are random. The same should be true for the craftsmen (i.e. not all craftsmen should be available each game).

I'll definitely try it again (partly because I own it, and partly because the board is so attractive), but so far I'm disappointed.

Eagerness to Play Again: Within a year

Plays (1):
2 players: 1

RATING: 6
Lots of choices to make, but the theme doesn't work for me.  It doesn't feel like we are actually building anything.  Feels like lots of activities to maximize VP.  Players who have played before have a significant advantage in knowing the future cards that come up in later rounds.

Hint for beginners, horde resources early.  Cash them out a little later in the game when the resource to VP ratios improve.  Don't worry about VP in the early turns, focus on building up money and resources.  Getting the trades people early who produce resources is valuable, especially the one that gives you a stone a turn.  The seer that let's you see the events is moderately valuable.

RATING: 8
Wow, what a cool game.  Sort of like Age of Empires 3, but less complicated.  I very much like that, once you gotten a round or two under your belt, the game flows along very smooth and quick.  Also, the rules are put together very well.  There is very little room for  misunderstanding, which means that very little time is spent arguing over interpretation.  Another plus is that there are only 6 rounds of play, and the game ends.  That way, it doesn't drag on for a long time.  I am liking this game more every time I play it.  I can't wait for the 5-6 player expansion.  That will allow this game to hit the table much more often on our game nights.

 The bits are wooden, and fairly ordinary (except for the Cathedral), but the cards are beautifully printed, and the board is absolutely gorgeous!  This game will definitely see some action at our game nights.

RATING: 7
It's a lot like Caylus but has more luck.  The worker-position and craftsmen are selected via draft.  The workers are used to generate cubes and the craftsmen are cards that basically give you your exchange rate in terms of cubes/victory-points.  However, players' builders (which compare to Caylus' workers) are pulled randomly out of a bag.  So especially you have to rely on a bit of luck to get the optimal spots on the board. Even going first does not guarantee that you won't get unlucky and get the worst 3 spots.  This can be harsh on the last turn and can flat out decide a game.  To me, that's the main weakness in an otherwise excellent game.  I give it a 7 because the game is a lot of fun and I think I would usually be willing to play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Beautifully presented game.  Players own a tribe or workers and craftsmen, and use them to turn coloured wooden cubes, and money, into victory points.  It's set against the backdrop of constructing a 12th century cathedral, which essentially is the game clock and start player marker.  A typical turn is workers head out and bring in money and goods (staples such as sand, rock and wood).  Then master builders are drawn randomly from a bag and allocated to a role for the turn.  Roles include visiting the King's Court for tax protection and a lump of metal, the market for more trading, etc.  Then the roles are resolved in ordered fashion, culminating in converting goods into other goods and the important victory points.  Quite a bit going on, but plays cleanly and quickly (probably 75 minutes once you've seen it), with that sharp pain of turn angst.  I'm not quite sure about the random drawing bit for the master builders... that may be a bit too random, but the game works well and should be good for a few more plays.

RATING: 7.55
One of the better, light worker placement games with ingenious mechanic for worker order selection. The game feels a bit scripted with having limited set of variables so it seems that it has limited number of replays (like [b]Agricola[/b] without occupations nor minor improvements). The board and the art is beautiful though.

[b]Favorite mechanism:[/b] When placing workers they are first pulled out of the bag, then the player whose worker color is extracted chooses to pay a lot to play that worker immediately or to play it later for free. Then the price lowers and the next worker is pulled out etc. The first player has a choice of veto: to exchange the worker he just pulled out for another one in the bag, once.

RATING: 5
We have yet another worker placement game.  Luck plays a role in this game, which I can live with but I feel is better handled in Stone Age.  What I mean by this is that the luck can be mitigated more in Stone Age most of the time.  I really don't like the luck involved with the drawing of the Master Builders and while I'm aware there is a variant in the expansion to address this I don't like the game enough to purchase it.  I feel you also have more control in Stone Age, for example trying to burn through a building stack to end the game.  In Pillars I don't feel like you have a lot of choices because the optimal one is usually apparent.  Some of the Privileges don't seem balanced.  This a lot of negative comments for something of this rating.  The reason being is that if I had played this before other games of it's ilk I would probably consider it's mechanisms novel and pretty fun regardless.  There are just a lot better options in the worker placement genre these days.

RATING: 9
An excellent offering that comes as a surprise when looks at the very few succesful book/film tie-ins that are out there (and especially Kosmos' speckled history in that respect). In fact the game mostly avoids a close representation of the book, which mostly is some flair text on the cards (and there is very little flair text). This is simply a very delightful and challenging game for gamers, with more than a passing resemblance to Caylus but with some aspects that make the game actually more accessible and even more forgiving. The different phases work very beautifully together, there is no downtime as all phases are interactive and the game also doesn't overstay it's welcome but is engrossing up to the end. The fancy turn counter (the cathedral IS the turn-counter, in case you wondered) made Richard Borg laugh so hard that he nearly fell out of the chair, when he watched our game. And it was a hearty laugh if there ever was one!
But seriously - if you like involved but not too heavy games with lots of beautiful bits and pieces  you should clearly consider purchasing this wonderful game. It was one of the very rare cases that a German company produced game was actually completely sold out at Essen, even though it was available in great numbers originally.
Very recommended!

RATING: 7
SOLD IT!
then REBOUGHT IT!

Good Worker placement game. Loved the master builder in a bag turn order system (refreshingly different). Felt a bit odd using my workers as "money" (place X workers to buy resources with X for a cost vs. assign 1 worker to get 1 action). Understand why the wooden blocks could be seen as a waste of upping the game cost, but I sort of enjoyed playing with them so I guess I liked having them. Found the rules for replacing start craftsmen fiddly (would rather reduce the number you can have to 4 and have the need to keep spending action to replace them the punishment rather then the listed negative effects... similar to replacing a power plant in power grid "there is no penalty, just the fact that you used money to replace one")

Recently played with only 2 people and found the experience to be a little flat with only minor player interaction, so best with more players.

RATING: 7
A worker placement game that seemingly does nothing really well, but (and much more importantly) does nothing poorly. What I mean by that is that its got strategy, but a little short of what Caylus has. It has randomization and optimization, but short of what Agricola or Le Harve has. It also has some more tactical scenarios (drawing of master builders), but it's definitely not a totally tactical game. What you have left is a well rounded worker placement game with no real flaws, but no gimmicks to make it stand out either. This is the first game that was off my radar I've played in a while that I think I could play 10+ times. Good game.

RATING: N/A
Based on Ken Follett's book "The Pillars Of The Earth".  Looks like a decent family game, spoken of as Caylus lite, and the optimizing of resources also reminds me of San Juan.  Less control with four players, but I was fairly impressed with my first two plays (3 and 4 player).  Beautiful components too.  Possible downsides: - too much analysis paralysis for two players? - too random for four players?  logistic game too similar to games we already have like San Juan and St Petersburg? 

Alternatives: [gameid=27364] - Card game version of Caylus, lighter and quicker, 2-4 players, although probably best with 3 or 4.  Basically a game of logistics and resource management.  Quite impressed after the first play (2-player), although it is a real economic euro game and it's not worth having too many in that category.  Not as pretty as Pillars of the Earth however.
[gameid=34635] - Equally beautiful (perhaps better) components, and similar mechanics and concepts, and slightly lighter.
Agricola is also similar in some ways, and perhaps is the best game to get of this sort.

RATING: 6
I had some fun sessions of PotE on BSW, but I wouldn't want to play on table relative to the other worker placement options available. On BSW, though, it's one of the better options. 

This is an interesting attempt to merge Caylus and Power Grid, or so it seems. I liked the Power Grid-ish worker cards, but the game is ultimately undone by its randomness and constrained play. Rather than players thwarting each other with thinky, tempo-stealing moves, this design prefers to have the game do it. With so many options out there for worker placement, you can find a tighter design than this one.

RATING: 8
I really like this game.  The gameflow is elegant and with a limit of three masterbuilders, the choices are difficult.  There is a big element of luck introduced with the drawing of the master builder pieces and with the order in which the crafter and event cards come out.  This can seriously mess with one's strategy, so one of the things that makes this game shine for me is the need to maintain flexibility. The master builder issue is mitigated somewhat with the placement rules in the expansion, which is now how we always play. 

Things do get pretty tight with four players, and sometimes the choices are not good with this number, a problem which is addressed with the expansion.

In sum, Pillars of the Earth is a great worker placement game that continues to grow on me, but it seems that everyone  else has moved on from this.

RATING: 7.5

Pillars is a good game that's fun to play and offers plenty of agonizing decisions!  Grade: B-

RATING: 7
This is a typical euro, with resource management, victory point conversion and a bit of luck thrown in for good measure. A couple of things seem wrong though. For one, I think there are too many different resources (good cubes, money, meeples, victory points) and some of them are almost equivalent (money vs meeple). Also, the action selection phase, with its randomness, can throw the game one way or the other during the last turns. Finally, the game just doesn't feel original. Developement is better here than in Leonardo da Vinci, but I'm not convinced it makes for a better game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pillars is a worker placement game that I'd place somewhere between Caylus and Stone Age in terms of both game weight and luck factor. Originally, I was a bit concerned about long-term value, especially due to the fact that there was a fixed set of artisans that would be used in each and every game so that while you could not be sure exactly when (or rather where) they appear it was certain they would do so eventually. Luckily, the expansions available seem to deal with this and I no longer expect this to be a problem.

The random turn order adds a lot of luck to the game, possibly a bit too much for its own good, but that, too, has been addressed in the expansion.

Update: As expected, replayability without the expansion is an issue. I'll always try to add the expansion; not necessarily the new board but the additional cards for certain.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.7
On first sight there are a lot of options but during the game it offers only few tactical decisions which seem to be compulsions of optimization.
 
Considering also the static course of the game, this is too few to make Pillars an outstandig game.
Furthermore I am concerned about two things: the scoring mechanism and significant luck influence (draw out of the bag).
The scoring mechanism because you will get in the last round nearly a thrid of VPs as you did in the 5 rounds before (balance issue). The last round is so important that you must be first player to be sure to get what you need. The luck of the draw (out of the bag) may destroy the advantages of your strategy so that all players end with only few VPs difference.
[i]It is too complex for a familiy game but has to much luck factor for strategic purists.[/i] So neither target group is satisfied throughout. However I still like the theme and atmosphere during the game but honestly I don't feel like building a Cathedral. The game doesn't live up its awards.

RATING: 8
Építészek „háborúja”
Kevés olyan játékkal találkozhat az ember, amelynek tervezésekor egy kiadott könyvet vettek alapul. Itt Ken Follett híres monumentális regénye, a Katedrális szolgáltatja az alapot, amely kis hazánkban is népszerűségnek örvend, sőt már sorozat is készült belőle, ha valakit elrettent a majd 1000 oldal elolvasása.
A regény szereplői, eseményei ebben a társasban visszaköszönnek ránk, így a mű olvasója könnyebben bele tudja élni magát a szerepébe. Maga a játéktábla is a legszebbek egyike, így nem csoda, hogy játék közben elkalandozhat a figyelmünk egy-egy részlet megtekintése közben.
A játékban a katedrális megépítését követjük végig (fix 6 fordulón keresztül), amelynek során munkásaink és mestereink segítségével építőanyagot, pénzt és győzelmi pontot gyűjtünk, privilégiumokat, támogatást szerzünk. Ezek közül a játék végén a győzelmi pontok mennyisége alapján kerül ki a győztes, de ahogy ez lenni szokott, a meglévő lehetőségeink legjobb kombinálása kell az elsőbbséghez.
Minden fordulóban 3 fázis játszunk végig. Az elsőben lehetőségünk van munkásainkkal építőanyagot megszerezni vagy a pénzünkből egyre jobb mestereket alkalmazni, akik a megszerzett építőanyagot győzelmi pontokká „alakítják” át. A játékosok egymás után következnek és ameddig van munkásuk választhatnak a kihelyezett kártyák közül.
Majd következik a legizgalmasabb rész, az építészeink táblára történő lehelyezése. A kezdőjátékos egy vászonzsákból sorban húzogatja ki a játékosok bábúit, majd azt az egyre kisebb költségű helyekre teszi le. Ha fizetsz a kis pénzecskédből, hamarabb cselekedhetsz, mint a többiek, de fogy a pénzed. Ha később következel, azt olcsóbban megúszod, de mi marad már neked? A táblán 14 helyszínből 9 helyszín egyikére (valamelyik helyszínen akár több lehetséges hely van, hogy több játékos is használja azt) teheted le az építészedet, amely helyszíneken jó és jobb dolgok történnek veled.
A harmadik fázisban pedig a lerakott munkások és építészek bábúit vesszük vissza és hajtjuk végre a helyszínek akcióit (játékos befolyásoló események kivédése, győzelmi pontok megszerzése, mesteremberek megszerzése, adómentesség, kezdőjátékos szerepének átvétele, piacolás az építőanyagokkal és így tovább…).
Minden forduló 3. fázisának 13. helyszínén, a Katedrálisban történik meg az eddig elvégzett, kitaktikázott munka gyümölcsének leszüretelése és a meglévő mestereid segítségével a győzelmi pontok bezsákolása. Általánosságban elmondható, hogy az építőanyagok közül az olcsóbbakból kevesebb pontod lesz, a drágábbakból több, viszont az olcsóbbakból többet tudsz egy fordulódban felhasználni, míg a drágábbakból kevesebbet.
A játékosok feladata jó építésvezetőként megtalálni az egyensúlyt a pénz-építőanyag-mesterek között.
Sajnos 2 főnél mindenképpen háziszabályok alkalmazása ajánlott, mert egyszerűen nincs versenyhelyzet, a játékélmény ezáltal kisebb is lesz. Egyes helyszínek is erősebbnek tűnnek a többinél, valamint eddig a kő és a kőfaragó mester kombója is elég erősnek mutatkozott.
Összességében egy szép, tematikus, bestseller regényre épülő játékot ismertem meg, amely a fenti hiányosságok ellenére is jó szórakozás.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
After 6 games with 2 players, i like very much but would like to point several things to improve:

-There are too few cards (specially artisan ones) and also the last round the "advantage" cards are always the same. Too easy to memorize and this goes against replayability.

-With standard rules we never had the need to place our master builders at the market (so now every round we dray 5 instead of 7 resource cards)

-Also, we almost never placed the builder on the "Next Initial Player", as with two players one rarely feels the need.

Overall, a funny game, even for 2 players.

RATING: 6.5
Un jeu grosso modo de cubes (placement de travailleurs) qui laisse place à une grande part de hasard parfois déconcertant avec la pige initiale des pions dans le sac de tissus. C'est facile de se confondre avec le nombre de cubes requis pour accomplir un objectif. C'est assez long et peu interractif. C'est un Euro qui ne se distingue pas vraiment des autres du genre: on ramasse des cartes qui nous donne des cubes en plaçant des ouvriers et ensuite on remet les cubes à la banque en réalisant des cartes d'objectifs qui vont nous amener à faire des points de victoire. Le thème n'est pas tellement accrocheur non plus. Le jeu est par ailleurs quand même assez esthétique. Ce n'est pas un jeu que j'achèterais. C'est le genre de jeux qu'on peut se lasser rapidement étant donné son absence de thème original fort.

RATING: 4
I really like the board graphics.  I liked the big church pieces as a time mechanism, any game bits that big are cool.  The theme was mostly absent.  We are each builders, working on a cathedral and getting VPs?  You have contractor dudes helping you (represented on cards) they give you the "exchange rate" of resource cubes to money or veeps.  You use money to hire new contractor dudes and you have some workers you use to mine/farm/lumberjack you resource cubes.  There are also master builders which take special actions every turn.  Not a bad basic idea, and a popular one in the world of euros.

The problem is that it boils down to yet another worker placement game where very few of your options are interesting and each turn you look around, place your guy in the least ass spot available, and go merrily on your way...  None of the decisions seem particularly tense or interesting, but occasionally a tough decision will be produced.  Did AOE:III spoil me such that few other worker placement games will satisfy?  

Also, How many times are we going to see the put in resource cubes, use your upgradeable building/worker/merchant etc. to produce vps game design?  I cannot see how this particular game would really have any kind of replayability, although it does belong in the "once every three months" euro collection model.  Most (all?) of the interaction is just who is going to get in line first for the shitty option they dislike the least.  Not my type of game at all...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Kind of like Caylus, but missing the very nice "player order isn't random except for the first turn" feature of Caylus.  Nice that everybody has the same number of Master Builders (workers), but the order each turn is a totally random grab bag (literally).

Six turns is exactly the right number for this game -- you can see where the game is going and make plans, but it doesn't take too long to finish.  Unlike Caylus (which has no randomness), analysis paralysis is minimized somewhat because of the random event cards, privilege cards, and craftsman cards coming out.  (Well, in the base game, all the craftsmen come out every turn, but some get taken as an action and some have to be paid for with gold, which randomizes things a lot.)
 
My first game was 2-player, which is good, but quite different -- not nearly as much conflict for actions.  I actually like 2-player, because you can be more strategic, and not have to hedge too much against the possibility that all the good actions might be taken by the time your next Master Builder gets drawn.

I also played 4- and 5-player (with the expansion) -- very crowded, and hard to score consistently.  But it's remarkable how people tend to specialize in their own path to points, which allows you to avoid other people's paths.

Overall, a fun game so far, and I wouldn't mind playing it every once in a while.

RATING: 9
 Pillars is nice to see, and even though the construction is a pretext (just like in Caylus), it feels a little less abstract (thanks to the literary reference and the beautiful board); what makes this a keeper is that it is way more easy to teach than Caylus: I played with my 10 years daughter with no problem, and she liked it. 
Note that the only commonality with Caylus is the worker placement mechanism, and it's implemented differently.
Not only the game is simple to play but the rules are written so that you can start playing without reading them completely.
The game becomes deeper as you are more acknowledged with the cards; then you can really establish strategies.  As these crafter cards are not that numerous, it is useful to show them when explaining the rules, showing for instance what can be done with gold and how to make many points at the end of the game; other wise, new players will be crushed and disgruntled with the game.
9 with exp.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]A BEAUTIFUL BOARD, BEAUTIFUL BITS, BEAUTIFUL CARDS, BEAUTIFUL STORY. THIS GAME SO FAR IS EASILY ONE OF MY FAVORITES, AND IT ALL GOT BETTER [I]AFTER[/I] I READ THE BOOK AND WATCHED THE MINISERIES. A MUST-HAVE FOR EVERY BOARD GAMER[/b]

The first time I played this game, I hated it, I didn’t understand what was happening and the whole worker placement idea of it made my head hurt. Too many decisions, too many layers of depth, all of them so foreign and strange to me, left me unfulfilled. I never wanted to play it again.

After realizing the game was based on a book that had received so great reviews and criticism, I look a bit into it, and finally read it. The story was amazingly elegant, the characters were complex enough to empathize with them and their backgrounds and the whole idea behind it all, the construction of the Kingsbridge cathedral, was to say the least epic. Building things that stand the test of time is a topic that has always interested me, and after reading the book, I started learning more about cathedrals and medieval buildings. I am not very religious, but cathedrals do have a certain air of sanctity and accomplishment. I had to try it out myself, if only through the minor experience of playing a master builder in the board game I had once hated.

The circle was complete. The co-op aspect of the game and between the players, all acting like master builders in the construction of a model cathedral (Good idea to have the pieces of a small wooden building to place on the board game after each round) is astonishing and it makes the players feel like they are working together in a humbling project and in a vital part of History. But in spite of that, the race for the best resources, the best artisans and the best places in the king’s court is what the game is all about.

Each player starts humbly in the same place, with the same possibilities, although with a minor difference in money, which is sometimes very important, but sometimes it means so very little, so thinking that money will win the game is not a certainty, but an ignorant man’s (Or woman) wishful thinking. After getting their hands on the best resource extracting sites, and hiring a few new artisans, the players will have a vast number of advantages from where to choose to place their pawns, each with a particular advantage that only that player will have, until better choices appear and the player decides he must change his outlook on his building project. Selling and buying at the supermarket, recruiting the help of the characters from the book or racing to be the first to get the precious metal cubes are only a few of these choices.

The game features also one of the best artwork and designs ever created, in my honest opinion. Michael Menzel did an amazing job with the board game illustration and the card drawings. Certainly, the game is worth buying for the art alone.

Easily, The Pillars of the Earth is one of my Top 5 games of all time.

[b][i]I. Criteria[/b][/i]

Mechanics: :d10-1::d10-0:
Design & Art:  :d10-1::d10-1:
Packaging & Presentation: :d10-9:
Theme: :d10-1::d10-0:
BGG Factor: :d10-8:

[b][i]II. Sliders[/i][/b]

Abstract [b]________[COLOR=#FF0000][9][/COLOR]_[/b] Themed: The round and turn system is common to many games, but the depth of precision within this game makes it difficult to separate certain stages of the round and turn from the theme of it. Maybe a game about pure administration could do well with almost every element within the game, but the ambient of the game is very well served from it’s theme, and viceversa. Also, the whole building of a medieval cathedral thing is way to strong to simply say this is an abstract game with a certain theme thrown in. The game was created through the implementation and constant presence of the theme, or so it looks to me. Theme... theme.
 
Simple [b]_________[COLOR=#FF0000][10][/COLOR][/b] Complex: A good game to me is one that’s easy to learn and hard to master. The endless menu of options and the limited amount of decision to be taken makes this game amazingly deep and intricate and a bad decision could echo through the rest of the game, so players are required to approach each decision with ease and calm, and every investment must be done hoping it will bring about fortune in the future. Playing while focused on one thing will certainly not win the game.

Strategic [b]__ [COLOR=#FF0000][3][/COLOR]_______[/b] Tactical: Players might think that the amount of decisions means tactical decisions will win this game. They wouldn’t be wrong, but strategies may pay off if they’re well executed and the events of the game and the decisions of other don’t affect them too much. Changing the strategy in the middle of the game may be a risky move, but experimentation could serve best the goals of the players. 
 
Luck [b]_______[COLOR=#FF0000][8][/COLOR]__[/b] Skill: Luck plays a very small part in this game, because there’s always one way or the other, sooner or later, to change fate or to avoid certain random dangers, like losing workers, money, resources or time. Waiting for the right moment (If possible) is a sound idea. 

Fiddly [b]_______[COLOR=#FF0000][8][/COLOR]__[/b] Elegant: The game is not perfect. A lot of questions will arise while playing the game, but they won’t be too big to bring the game to a halt. The most common questions and doubts about the game can also be solved with logic and some application of the same principles the game is based on, but playing with newbies with the rule book close at hand is always a good idea..
 
Indirect interaction [b]_______[COLOR=#FF0000][7][/COLOR]___[/b] Direct interaction: Players may decide to use certain pawns to block other players, but a quiet and nice game can be reached too, with the occasional misstep here and block there. As the number of players increase, so will the need to be a little bit more ruthless will appear. 

Cutthroat [b]_____[COLOR=#FF0000][6][/COLOR]____[/b] Friendly: Like I said before, plying to win does not mean playing to destroy the competition. Sometimes, playing for the fun of it and not minding what the other players do could very well be the best decision, and with a good group of friends, this game can be a true delight, almost completely free of feuds and races between master builders..

Heavy use of language [b]_____[COLOR=#FF0000][6][/COLOR]____[/b] No language needed: The very few cards that use text are easy to understand and apply, sometimes there will be doubts about how certain term works or what a precise word means within the context of the game, but usually, those doubts are not really that difficult to solve. The game in that sense uses a lot of language, but certain terms are easy to understand.

[b][i]III. Number of players recommended[/i][/b]: 4.

[b]CHEERS![/b]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
First played January 20th, 2010. Taught in 15 minutes, played in 1.5 hours with 3 players. This has good strategy and luck balance. The game last 6 rounds. The initial start player (random) is given 20gold and each following player is given 1 more gold. Each player is given 2 VPs, 3 master builders, 3 initial craftsmen (cards), and 8 workers (one is larger and counts as 5). The board is set with resources wood (forest), stone (quarry), sand (beach), metal (king court), extra workers (castle), 12 (4 of each: wood, stone, sand goods; trading post), event card, 2 privledge cards, and 2 crasftmen cards.  An additional 2 craftsmen cards along with 6 goods cards are placed for players to begin bidding workers for starting with the start player. The player pays $(for crastmen upgrades; limit 5 total including starting 3) or workers for goods. Players may also choose to place workers in the town to get $1 each worker. Next the start player pulls a materbuilder from a bag and places it on a cost wheel (starts at $7). The start player may also once per round re-draw one pawn at any time. Players may choose to place their pawn in the castle(2 workers for next round), trade goods(buy), ignore event, upgrade crastmen, be start player next turn(if not chosen order remains the same), take a privlege card(extra good, etc), 1-2 VPs, go to king's court(first there gets a metal, all are exempt from tax), or pass. The cost goes down one for each sucessive pawn drawn until zero is reached. All pawns after and previously passed pawns placement cost is $0, in order origianlly draw. The event card is revealed(lose goods, master builder, or other hardship). Next players use their craftsmen to turn goods into $ or VPs. Board cards are replenished and the next round begins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pillars of the Earth is an awesome middle/light weight worker placement game with terrific production value and excellent mechanics. The flow of the game works great--the down time between turns in Pillars of the Earth is pleasantly small; players are constantly engaged in the game. The cards that allow rules to be broken are very interesting. Players are continually making interesting choices. I only have one play logged, but I imagine I wouldn't want to play repeatedly for an extended time-- a terrific game to add to regular rotation and a game I would want to keep in my collection for a long time. 

Probably the most outstanding thing about Pillars of the Earth, besides the super cool production value, is the mechanic where players take turns placing master builders on the board to draft actions. Each player has three master builders(action pawns), and everyone's master builders are randomly drawn from a bag one at a time by the start player. The first master builder drawn must choose between paying 7 money to draft an action or pass. Then the next drawn must choose between paying 6 money and pass. This continues until all the MBs are drawn(actions become free after seven are drawn) when the bag has emptied, any passed MBs, then get to choose actions for free in the sequence they passed. That mechanic is awesome. The decisions are very interesting and it makes the game exciting and fun. The start player has the privilege of once a round replacing a drawn MB into the bag, like if he had drawn first and didn't want to pay 7 or if a bunch had been drawn and there is an actin he really wants and he didn't pull his own MB. This action drafting mechanic is excellent and adds a significant fun factor to the game play.

RATING: 8
Pillars of the Earth is a very good game, but a little strange for me for reasons that I can't quite explain. The experience of playing it is very intriguing; the game has a unique character to it. It's much different than most games I own/play. The theme is very different, but surprisingly well represented through the mechanics. I'm not very good at the game, but I still find it entertaining. It's quite beautiful to look at, and one's choices within the game have incredible weight, giving the game a very tense undertone. Did you mess up and not get the most out of your extra worker on the third turn? That's definitely going to come back to haunt you. This lends the game to a little bit of analysis paralysis, but I don't think to a crippling degree. 

It has a sort of timeless quality to it that is hard to define. It doesn't get played much these days, and I'm not sure why.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
First game with 4 players. Pillars of the Earth is a well-designed game that works smoothly and has attractive components. However, like many a recent mid-weight Euro game (Notre Dame is a prime culprit), the theme just does not matter. OK, so Pillars is not quite as bad as Notre Dame in this respect but isn't PotE supposed to be based on a novel? If this were the game's only fault then I would still rate it an 8. But the element of chance is also a bit of a turn-off. I have no problem with the random selection of the various cards and the rolling of the die to determine the level of tax. But there is THE BAG!!! Twice I used the start player special ability to put one of my master builders back in the bag and drew...another of my master builders. UPDATE: I have played 3 or 4 times now and I appreciate the game more. The bag can still be annoying, but I'm prepared to accept it.

RATING: 7
Die Säulen der Erde is a very nice medium-weight game, not as light as the term 'family game' might imply, but not as deep or convoluted as the heavy-weighter it is most often (rightly) compared to, Caylus. The game feels quite friendly with two players, with not so much in the way of direct competition for scarce resources; with more players, competition becomes much more pronounced. Heartily recommended; at the moment my favorite game from this year's Essen. My only real complaint is that the mechanism for drawing master builders from the bag seems too random, if one is trying to play this as a "real" strategy game.

2017-03-21

I managed to play Pillars of the Earth again and it was a very disappointing experience. Now, I feel it is way too light for my current tastes, but what I really dislike in the luck with the bag. In our last game my meeples were taken out of the bag 4 times in a row, even though I put one of them in the bag again. I feel this luck is way too unbalancing and uncontrollable.

Also, there is not anything interesting to do at the end of each round. All the best sport are already taken.

Bottom line, The luck of the draw is too much for me. I will do my best to stay away from this game. I concede it can be a good game to introduce to new players to our hobby. Pillars of the Earth is an average and overrated game, albeit very pretty.

RATING: 8
The game unfolds the story of building a cathedral.

Players manage their money for paying labourors or buying the services of artisans. Labourors bring in three different resources which the artisans can convert to browny points for contributing to the cathedral.

Not a lot of player interaction... the interaction is the "I bought something you wanted before you could get to it" variety, but unlike some games in this style there's a fairly good range of usefulness of what's on offer, so the competition is decent.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

2010
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
- One of my favourite gateway games.
- I'm not sure why, but I really like the aesthetics of the game.
- There's a nice mix of choices, tactics and strategy here. Do you go for a strategy that will produce points early and consistently, or do you go for bulk points later in the game?
- The drawing of pawns from the bag can be both a blessing and a curse. In early rounds, you might not want to be drawn early because of the increased cost. In later rounds you might not care about the cost and desperately want to go sooner in the round.
- Pretty easy to teach, and a great introduction to worker placement, resource management, etc.
- Great with all player counts.

RATING: 10
I've played Pillars countless times now and I'm not afraid to rate it a 10. I win Pillars against my family 95% of the times we play, and I can assure you that this game has luck, but is not based on it. The ability to adapt your strategy to random events will always win the day, and there is a feeling of satisfaction everytime you get above (bad) luck.

If you are skillful with maths (as I am) you will have a clear advantage. And that means fun to me, and my game-mates have fun too trying to beat me all the time.

I've been involved in many unlucky situations (my workers drawn last, no money-maker card for me, etc) and still I've won those games. Swear.

This game is fun in many levels and I highly recommend it.

RATING: 8
For a reasonably heavy game, Pillars of the Earth is quite enjoyable to play.  There are many routes to victory.  The values of things in the game change depending on how many rounds are left, and the circumstances of the players.  The graphical quality of the game is amazing.  I play on BSW, but the physical game board looks even better.  Plenty of room for skill, but not overwhelming for beginners, since you can kind of play your own game.  Many similarities with Caylus (everything I have said so far also applies to Caylus!), and the craftsman feature is kind of like the power plants in Power Grid.  Unfortunately, many of the craftsmen are pretty bad, so you end up using only about 2/3 of them.  Also, the cost track doesn't go up high enough in my opinion, and if there is a great craftsman available, the person lucky enough to have their master builder drawn out of the bag first will get it.  Maybe this last criticism is slightly unfair, since the players can earn the right to go first by choosing the cathedral with one of their MBs, but having all those useless bricklayers and mortar-mixers surely could have been avoided with better playtesting.  Even so, this game has a lot going for it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Típico juego de colocación de trabajadores, visualmente muy atractivo.

Se trata de ir construyendo una catedral mediante la aportación de recursos, aunque en ningún momento da esa sensación.

Al principio de cada turno se consiguen cartas de mejora o de recursos, mediante dinero o trabajadores. Entonces comienza el turno, en el que cada jugador tiene 3 peones que saldrán al azar de una bolsa y que irán determinando el orden de juego y la cantidad de dinero que se pagará por cada acción. Una vez hecho esto, se resuelve el turno según el orden del tablero y al final del turno se cambian recursos por puntos de victoria en una supuesta costrucción de la catedral.

Me parece un juego más de este estilo, que es entretenido para jugar con no jugones. En ningún momento se siente sensación de agobio por no tener dinero, y además, como las cartas se guardan de turno a turno, siempre se van a tener unas acciones mínimas garantizadas, que sólo pueden mejorar. Por último, me parece que el azar de la bolsa de peones puede echar al traste más de una estrategia.

El juego es entretenido, pero está a años luz de Caylus.
[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2010
[/color]
