RATING: 7
This is a game that was high on the list of "Essen 2006 favorites," and (until recently) I would have had it rated higher, probably an 8.  Experience playing the game in competitive tournament environments like WBC suggests that, while the game might not be "broken," like St. Petersburg, there are issues (1) regarding the relative merits of particular Craftsmen -- i.e., some may be inherently better than others and (2) a tremendous luck factor in the random pulls from the bag.  Replay value, which I initially thought might be quite high, is not that great given that it's the same set of Craftsmen each turn, and it appears that certain strategies seem to always do better -- i.e., get the Money Woodworker on Turn 1, take Start Player on the turn before the really good Craftsmen come out, make sure you get early money (true in alot of similar games, etc.) Now, a recent expansion does go a long way towards correcting these problems, but the learning curve in teaching both the regular game and then the expansion on top of it means the game just isn't hitting the table that often, some two years after the original first appeared.  While I like Pillars better than some of the less interesting Euros such as Caylus (that can drag on a bit), it's just not that interesting any more -- whereas other Essen 2006 releases such as Imperial, Through the Ages and Leonardo da Vinci are still somewhat fresh and new even after repeated plays.

RATING: 4
I was slow in getting around to Pillars of the Earth. Early comparisons to Caylus convinced me that I should wait - I already liked Caylus, so I wanted to play it some more before checking out Pillars. I needn't have bothered. In Caylus you get the satisfaction of watching a town spring to life. Decisions matter thematically and mechnically; some towns will gentrify early, some will delay moving to stone construction, etc. In Pillars the Cathedral comes together piece by piece (a mere six pieces) no matter what players do; in fact, everyone could go bankrupt and start losing points and that silly cathedral would still rise up in the center of the board. Other quibbles: the game gives you a whole bunch of workers but has you place them in fairly large groups, so you're actually taking very few actions. The actions you do take amount to hiring craftsmen that all do the same thing: convert resources into points (at better rates as the game goes on). The idea is that you're throwing more and more skilled labor and resources at the Cathedral, but in practice you just draft some cards, grab some cubes, and trade those cubes for points based on your cards. It's like Power Grid with a less dynamic resource market... and almost nothing else (board position, player order, etc). Repeat a few times until game end with minor tactical decisions (the board offers a handful of extremely minor supplements, which are themselves attained via a surprisingly random mechanism - the much maligned master-builder draw).

EDIT: Played again about a year later. Confirmed as a very weak game. Take the procedural nature of Caylus and the power-plants of Power Grid, deflate of all depth, inflate with a lot of air, throw in a volatile level of randomness, and you've got Pillars: A ridiculous exercise in one-step cube-to-point conversion. I just don't get what people see in this. My God what a limp game.

RATING: 8.5
This is probably the perfect example of a middle-weight game. It has just the right amount of depth to be fulfilling, but is easy and light enough that it doesn't get bogged down in analysis-paralysis.

Players take turns programming their actions on the board and trying to maximize their VPs per turn. There's nothing too fancy about the mechanisms, except for the fact that player order is random (offset by a higher cost the earlier you play). 

Artwork on the board was great, but the bright colored meeples kinda ruined the muted painting.

RATING: 7

This is another great worker placement game. I love the structured, multi-phase rounds, the resource gathering mechanism, the different actions, and the Power Grid-like artesan cards, with disadvantages if you get rid of some. It just feels so good to play this game, to see everything you go through in advance. I like the order in this game, but my favorite mechanism is the one adding chaos to the mix: the drawbag turn order. It's just so clever: being picked early gives you the choice between placing now for a premium, or placing first of the non-payers; being picked later costs less, or you can decide to place last; then, being picked late is free and you place them then and there (after those who paid a lot, before those who chose not to pay). It rewards adaptability, there's some nice push your luck in there.

This game is so much like Puerto Rico it hurts, though rather than "roles" you select different aspects of cathedral production to work on, there are fewer commodities which also fulfil the role of colonists, and there aren't quite so many ways of accumulating VPs, though that's not to say that there aren't plenty of strategies for winning. I won this the first time I played it by pouncing on a couple of pricey craftsmen that give you VPs every turn and turn wood into coins early on. It meant I was broke for a little while, but I was able to come back strong later. All in all, it's a fun game, a little simpler than PR. There are some odd production omissions. Player mats are sorely needed. The board, though beautiful, looks disorganized and muddled. The cathedral pieces are nice, but I was also left with a question: if you're supposed to put the roof on in the beginning of the sixth turn, why is the game still continuing? The cathedral's already been built! Maybe I misread the rules, and since the cathedral pieces are just for flavor, it's nothing that matters; just another glitch in this game's matrix. I would definitely play it again, but it's not as elegant as PR, and owning both would be a waste.

RATING: 9
The Good: This is a fun strategy game that plays faster than most of it's peers. The board has beautiful artwork that really fits the theme well.Lots of good decisions to be made each turn, yet with little analysis paralysis.

The Bad: Some may complain about luck. Luck is present due to deciding turn order by pulling pieces out of a bag. However, the early pieces out of the bag must pay extra for their turn. I believe this works fairly well in a 2 player game. However, I feel that the luck of the draw plays more of a factor with more than 2.

RATING: 3
Beautiful components with a rather familiar gameplay: collecting cubes and transforming these cubes into victory points via worker cards while making sure not to run out of money. The fixed order for the craftsmen reduces the replayability. The main element of luck comes from the way the master craftsmen are drawn each round and when you are unlucky, then the best spots are already taken (I would prefer a mechanism in which you take an action to impact the playing order, e.g. like is done in Age of Empires III). The game tension/suspense is low (e.g. there is no urge to build anything special or to prevent your workers from starving). In summary: the game is too long for what it is and too luck based for what it wants to be. Don't enjoy this one at all.

RATING: N/A
At the beginning of the 13th century, construction of the greatest and most beautiful cathedral in England begins. Players are builders who try to contribute the most to this cathedral's construction and, in so doing, score the most victory points. Gameplay roughly consists of using workers to produce raw materials, and then using craftsmen to convert the materials into victory points. Workers may also be used to produce gold, the currency of the game. Players are also given three master builders each turn, each of which can do a variety of tasks, including recruiting more workers, buying or selling goods, or just obtaining victory points. Getting early choices with a master builder costs gold, as does purchasing better craftsmen. Players must strike a balance between earning gold to fund their purchases and earning victory points.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
(Die Saeulen der Erde)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
[2007.May.24] This resource conversion game is a gorgeous production that very nicely mirrors the book that it is based upon. It drips with theme, but is also a compelling game. As stated, the object of the game is simple: convert your resources to victory points. However, since luck is an issue--especially during craftsmen/resource card and master builder placements--players should be flexible with their strategies in order to be able to adjust them mid-game.

The game handles 2- and 3-players exceptionally well, but may play a bit too long for some with 4-players. (Perhaps with non-AP-prone players this may not be the case.)

[2016.Feb.2] Still a dependable "go to" in my collection, even after all these years. The expansions certainly gave it longer legs.

:star::star::star::star::nostar:

RATING: 9.5
Very, very nice game.  A rare balance of strategy, tactics, reacting, planning and randomness.  Playable with almost any group and enjoyable by players with vastly different levels of experience.  Under the hood it's a worker placement game with resource gathering, construction & feeding of your simple personal victory point engine (worker cards), with a nice, well integrated, theme.  (The game actually led me to discover the books, and not the other way around.)

It flows well, everyone I explained it to got it, it's pleasant to all levels of players, keeps everyone interested throughout and has an ok length.  This is my top rated game despite me not being able to identify or pinpoint a clear reason.  It's just one of the top, best constructed, play experiences around.  Obviously you can find better games for a set group, but in absentia, you can't go wrong with Pillars.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Beautiful board and solid play.  This is another efficiency game all about maximizing your actions.  Each turn consists of 3 phases: 1. Purchasing craftsmen OR assigning workers to a quarry, forest, or sand pit, 2. 3 masterbuilders from each player are mixed and randomly drawn from a black bag which then are placed (at cost which reduces by 1 gold for each masterbuilder placed and begins at 7 gold to place first) at various spots on the board which allows special actions during phase 3, 3. Going through the board and completing 14 mini-phases which is basically handing out free stuff or actions depending on where you placed your master builders.  After my first play, it feels light in nature but with quite a few difficult decisions as you seek to maximize your opportunities to score victory points.  I did not find the German language edition to be all that much of a problem as its easy and quick to refer to the translations for the cards.  However, the replayability of this game died in a hurry for me as it soon felt like there were only a couple tracks to victory and not many dynamic changes at any point in the game other than in the random master builder draw.

RATING: 6
Many people know that I am no Eurogamer. Based on the story, this Eurogame has beautifully illustrated components and a very standardize game mechanic - you resolve what happens in that medieval "Church" age one by one - events by the God, tax collection, taking special characters (advantage cards) or more efficient craftsmen (privilege cards), resources production, calling for more workers, commercial activities in market and then deciding who is going to be the new starting player. This is the kind of game more suitable to the wargamer like me, favoring a more clear and structured sequence of play. 

The game basically is about scoring victory points along the way, producing resources for the Church building earns you victory points. You can also "buy" victory points with goldsmith (and I like this mechanic very much!). I was in the lead for a long time but in the last turn a decision not to take the privilege with the master builders in Shiring (yep, I don't know why the place is called so but it is in the game anyway). John had been monopolizing the metal resources and in the end, won the game with a higher grade goldsmith. 

The game is not necessarily competitive among the players but how one gets the job done the most efficient way. It is not as abstract as other mathematical Eurogame, although I still feel some parts of the game are not so much related to the theme but just a way to proceed the game. I can't say it is a good game because I am not immensely satisfied with the completion of the game, but I can't say it either it is not a good game because of its clean, simple and straightforward mechanic. Well, it really depends on the liking of individual gamer after all.

RATING: 4
A "make cubes into something else" game. This is the accurate explaining of the game, really...

All the mechanics don't come together at all, it seems you're just getting cubes without any strategic decision at all. The luck (a thing I really like) really hurts this game, the "pull a pawn off a bag" mechanic seems utterly useless, it just gives randomness to a game that doesn't need/want more. The board and the art are beautiful, but it makes the game more confusing and even more pointless, just a charade to cover the game suckness. I've never thought I say this, but the game plays itself, after making 1-2 decisions to none, the game quickly becomes a machine telling you that you suck at making decisions. There's so much to think about and at the end all you have to do is: 

Place workers
Plan how to get cubes
You get Cubes
You trade Cubes for VP's

This over 6 rounds of play made me want to puke. I tried again to give it another chance... and its just the same.

This 4 is because of the art, and nothing more

RATING: 4
Possibly one of the prettiest games I own. Unfortunately initial plays left me a little flat. The randomness of the master builders was too much for my gaming buddies. The programed nature of the game didn't allow for the creativity I like when playing games, which results in a certain lack of control. (Do you play the game, or does it play you?)

In retrospect, I wonder if we were playing this game all wrong. The game presents itself as a gamer's game with an elaborate board, many bits, and lots of steps per turn. In reality, however, I wonder if this was supposed to be a fairly light game in a fancy package? I will need to play again to confirm.

Update: 
Too much rule explanation for non-gamers. Too much randomness for gamers. 

Traded.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Despite the artwork and theme, the actual gameplay felt very dry and mechanical---conceptually, it felt a lot more like [GAMEID=9217] or [GAMEID=36218] (games we disliked) rather than [GAMEID=31260], despite sharing a similar worker placement mechanism.  I think the problem for us was that in [GAMEID=24480], the resources have no differentiation in function---they could be anything.  You're just mechanically turning some random resource into points at some point in the game.  The resources themselves don't actually do anything as they do in [GAMEID=31260], where you can build a resource engine and then get victory points.  Although both schemes end up at the same place, it's that middle step of actually building something that we really like---in [GAMEID=31260], you have to get specific resources to build specific resource engines that then earn you points.  For example, we also really like games like [GAMEID=278] and [GAMEID=28143], which also contain these elements.  In contrast, in [GAMEID=24480], there's nothing to distinguish one resource from another.  As she said after the game, "It doesn't matter what the resources are.  They might as well be fish for all that it matters".  There was no more connection to the resources---it was merely an abstract, accountant's optimization exercise.  This is where I understood the term "cube pushing".

It also felt like there weren't too many actual decisions in the game.  It's usually the same resource cards that pop up and the same craftsmen that are available each game.  You get a lot of workers, but that's deceptive as most of the resource cards require a large number of workers anyway.  You also only get three master builders for the real worker placement mechanism.  Don't get me wrong, there are definitely decisions that you can optimize to win the game, but it just felt dry and less interesting to us.

While she really didn't like it, I tolerated it.  However, ultimately any time we would bring this out, we would just rather play [GAMEID=31260] instead.

RATING: 8
An excellent rhythm and structure to the game which eases you through what appears at first to be quite a daunting experience and turns Pillars of the Earth into compelling gameplay.

As the game was explained to us I could only stare at the text-heavy rule book and think they must of just extracted the book.  However, after one round was played everyone, knew what was going on and further reference to the rule book was not needed.

It must be said that we were a good group to test a game out.  4 of us, including 3 good players and 1 bad (me).  3 fast and 1 slow (not me).  3 battling for the lead and 1 watching it at a safe distance from behind (me).  Each player though, it appeared, enjoyed the game whatever the skill level, speed of turns and position on the scoreboard.  Enough decisions to be involved but not enough to paralyse.  Elements of randomness but still enough control to focus on tactics and have a hint of overall strategy.

A very good game which with a good explainer of rules could be a viable choice for most groups.

RATING: 4
Played on BSW. Fundamentally a nice design, but a couple of highly unnecessary and annoying inelegancies, especially the three craftsmen that have odd powers, spoil this otherwise highly engaging heavyweight. In fact, they are annoying enough to drop Pillars from a must buy to the bottom of my might want list. Still, I'm willing to continue playing, at least on BSW, for now.

Comparisons to Caylus, as such comparisons tend so often to be, are superficial (based largely on the master builder placement mechanic and the building-a-large-medieval-structure theme), and ultimately unenlightening.

UPDATE: Haven't really been playing this, although I bear it no real ill will. Nevertheless, I'm dropping my rating a point due to lack of interest.

UPDATE: I just played this again on BSW for the first time in a while, and time away has not done it any favours. I don't think there's anything wrong, exactly, with worker placement as a mechanism, but playing Pillars again just showed me that no one has tried anything more than throwing it in with a melange of loosely connected, unfocused, resource management mechanics (CAVEAT: I haven't played Leonardo daVinci or Agricola). I think a good game designer could make something fun of this, but I have yet to see it.

RATING: 8
Purely mechanical exercise with a theme that provides the appropriate atmosphere but has little to do with the actual game play. There's a great big cathedral in the center and I am ... earning Victory Points? Oh. How about at least calling them Prestige?

The theme drips all around everything, though none ever gets on you directly (so you won't need to wear a smock). However, this 'mechanism in search of a game' which is 1 part [GameID=555] and 1 part [GameID=9217], is *good*. It's a good length, with great action choices and lush bits.

I think 3 players might be 'optimum', but won't swear to it (good with any number).

RATING: 7
This worker placement game never really seems to take advantage of its -- well advantages. Part of this is the design decisions made by the publisher/designer. The wooden cathedral in the game serves only as a round marker. Goods are converted directly into VP, robbing the players of any sense of accomplishment. There are so many places where the game could have been enhanced with the inclusion of a simple rule or description. As worker placement games go, this is an okay one, but I think there are much better ones out there.

RATING: 8
Pillars of the Earth is a worker placement game with resource collection.  You allocate a number of workers to collect various resources and then you draw builders from a bag and can place them earlier if have the money to pay for it.  Otherwise you defer your turn until later.  You acquire buildings to transform resources into parts of a Cathedral that you build during the game.  The board is one of the best boards every made and you actually have little wooden pieces as a turn tracker.  

The game will remind you have Stone Age if you have played Stone age but it is just slightly heavier than Stone Age.  A little heavier than family weight but if you kids have played games this is a one that is great for 10 and up.

RATING: N/A
With a 2 hour playtime this might not be the best game until heavier/longer games have been played more frequently.

On the other hand, Tom Vasel's review at RPG sings the praises for this game (and reinforces how it's a great Caylus lite in it's easier and quicker play): "Few games blow me away with positive impressions as Pillars of the Earth did. Not only do I consider it one of the best games of 2007 – award worthy, even – but also it could easily become one of my favorite games. Playing well with two to four players, Pillars of the Earth has a building mechanic that takes place over six rounds, giving players choices similar to the smash hit Caylus, but in a quicker timeframe. I enjoy the escalating game play, and the games I’ve played have stayed close and tense throughout the entire game. Pillars of the Earth may look like a hodge podge of typical game components and mechanics, but it really is a special breakout, and I can see it becoming quite popular due to easy game play (enhanced by the theme) and tremendous interaction. "


See http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/436624

It seems that people are torn between this vs. Stone Age. It seems that it isn't worth owning if one already has Stone Age (and esp. if one isn't necessarily looking for more in depth game play than Stone Age; which PotE seems to be).

Caylus and Agricola are more engaging versions this game.

Nicer looking board than Notre Dame. Which says a lot considering that Stone Age really is a hit due to it's gorgeous game board (which serves to draw you into the theme).

CONCLUSION: Worth getting (before Notre Dame - see my comments there) if the price is right.

POST-SCRIPT: Maybe not worth getting at all. 
See: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/441850/family-focus-reviews-the-pillars-of-the-earth esp. with the following summary: "The Pillars of the Earth is a decent worker placement game that perhaps just tries too hard. The artwork is beautiful, the production value is good, and the mechanic behind the game is one of my favorites. However, the implementation of this mechanic is complex and clunky. This one flaw, for my family, greatly detracts from the fun factor of the game. I would recommend against The Pillars of the Earth for a casual gaming family. Instead, I would suggest Stone Age (as mentioned above), a more simplistic take on the same genre…and a more family friendly alternative while maintaining a strategic gameplay style which can be enjoyed by everyone."

NB: I suspect that there are many more games to play out before this one will become a staple in the game library

RATING: 7
12/2007  After a third game, now with most rules played correctly, this game is still going strong.  Very well balanced - where subtle stragegies, selections and flexibility (and a little luck) can give you the upper hand.  The average game will clock in at around two hours.  Time flies though as everyone is occupied and engaged all the time.  Looking forward to further plays.

5/2009 6 player game with expansion.  The expansion is a good addition making room for the added players.  It's a longer game but still within acceptable limits.

Interest for the game has fallen with time.  There's a feeling of the game playing you rather than you playing the game.  There's very little effort distinguishing victory and a mediocre result.Rating down 8->7.

RATING: 7
Pillars is a lot less complicated that the number of bits it includes might suggest, and it's a ton of fun to play. The components are beautiful, and it evokes the book quite well -- but is just as enjoyable if you've never read the book.

The turn order, taxation, and role selection are well implemented, and they combine to make every turn a rich decision with a good amount of room to be surprised by how things shake out. Just a solid game all around.

---

Years later, I still haven't played this again. I own other games similar to it that I'd rather play. Great game, though! Sold it.

RATING: 7
Just base game notes here. 

I like the game, the theme, the artwork and the experience. I definitely like having all the options and the little bit of variety from the card mechanics. However, I simply cannot stand the turn order mechanism. Many have noted that here and I can’t even believe that ever passed play-testing. That, and the length of the game, really ruin it. 

It has a decent learning curve and the first time can bog, but repeat plays are good. Yet, as much as I like this, for some reason I’m left with a feeling of “that wasn’t as much fun as the time invested”. I can’t put my finger on it just yet. Still, if you can find a copy cheap and are looking for something new, then you could do worse.

RATING: 8
Moni
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I was very happy to finally be able to add this game to my collection. It has a really cool worker placement approach, all of your workers go into a cloth bag, and are then drawn one at a time. The first worker drawn has first rights to go first, but it is expensive to go first, so passing becomes an option. If you pass, however, you are guaranteed to be one of the last to go, but it won't cost you anything. Each player has three workers to randomly go through this process with.

Also, the round marker is a wooden cathedral that is put together to signal that the game end is near. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
A really neat worker placement game, which basically have two separate worker placement segments within a single game round, as you first send your workforce to retrieve resources or to generate income, then there's a bag drawing sequence where as your tokens are pulled out, you may choose to pay money to place it and secure you the spot you need right away, or to wait and later place it for free, but the spot you want the most might already be taken.

Each round resolves the actions in a set order and culminate in another section of the cathedral being built.

RATING: 7
This game will always have a place in our hearts, as it was the game that got us hooked on modern gaming.  The first year we owned this we just couldn't get enough of it.  The game looks nice, it has a Catholic theme, and introduced us to the the joy that is resource management games.  If I had rated it back then it would have received a much higher score.  Having played other games, we find this game less balanced.  There are certain cards such as Prior Philips that if one of us receives it early in the game we are almost guaranteed the victory.  It got to the point that if this card showed up in the first three rounds we replaced it with a different card.  We play most of our games two player, so this is the basis for the majority of my ratings.  With two the amount of choices seem too much for your master builders, and with four the options seem too scarce.  Also, the actions you take don't seem to relate to building the Cathedral, whereas in some other games all of your actions make intuitive sense with the goal of the game, such as the concept of establishing a farm in Agricola (yeah, that's my favorite game).  Thinking of this game will always bring with it a sense of warmth and nostalgia, as it was the gateway to so much joy in our lives.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
2017.08.: Ez annyira német volt, hogy fél év után bontatlanul eladtam. Adja magát a kérdés, hogy akkor mi a rongyosra kopott dobozsarok miatt vettem meg?! Pánik. Valószínűleg pánik lehetett, hogy OoP a doboz és gyorsan akkor legyen meg inkább németül. Hibáztam. Láttam pár hete angol kiadását valahol. Boldogság.

Update: Persze miután eladtam, vettem csak észre, hogy az új JEM-ben ott vannak a magyar kártyák szépen beszerkesztett, nyomdakész pdf-ben...

2017.11.: Megvettem újra a német verziót. Ennyi előjáték után, ha nem fog tetszeni, komolyan dühös leszek a német társasjáték iparra.

2018.01.: Hosszú előjáték után végre kipróbálásra került (magyarított kártyák kinyomtatva, mi bajom lehet?!).

A játék bonyolultnak tűnhet elsőre a sok helyszín miatt, de gyorsan átláthatóvá válik. Az illusztrációk szépek, a tábla igazi Menzel-munka, ötletes a bábuk megkülönböztetése (kis munkás, nagy munkás, építész, fekete munkás, szürke munkás). A mechanizmusok közül az építészek lehelyezése egyedi, máshol (még) nem láttam ezt a megoldást.

A játék egyébként sajnos elég száraz, A-t váltunk át B-re vagy C-t B-re, esetleg D-t A-ra, vásárolunk olyan kártyákat, amikkel kedvezőbb átváltási arányokat érhetünk el. Hiába vannak korhű elnevezések (pl. üvegfúvó), ha ugyanúgy Y nyersanyagból csinál X pontot. Persze szinte minden eurós játék ilyen, talán az is baj, hogy ezt kb. 200 másik eurós után ismertem meg.

A játék szárazságán sajnos az eseménykártyák és előnykártyák sem változtatnak sokat, mert ezekből meg olyan kevés van, hogy hiába marad ki minden játékból 4 db, attól még mindig ugyanazok a kártyák kerülnek majd elő.

Jó iparosmunka, de semmi több. Anno, mikor megjelent, biztosan a legjobb eurós játékok közt emlegették (joggal). Ettől függetlenül nem volt rossz vele játszani, de már vannak ennél jóval szórakoztatóbb eurók is... Ennek ellenére még napjainkban is színvonalas játéknak számít.

Ha valaki egyszer azt kérdezi majd, hogy mondjak neki egy igazi klasszikus, ízig-vérig eurós játékot, ami még szép is, ez lehet az egyik, amit javasolni fogok. Főleg, ha Ken Follett rajongó.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Pillars plays in three phases. There's a fairly standard phase of card purchase, followed by a worker placement phase, and then a scoring phase where you take earned resources and convert them to points. The three parts of the game tie together nicely. I really enjoy how workers are pulled from a bag in the second phase. Earlier draws need to pay a higher fee for placing their worker, or pass and possibly lose out on high value spaces, but place later for free. All together, it seems nicely balanced with several possible routes to victory.

RATING: 9
The book is a must read and the game stayed "relatively" close to the book.  This a very good game with intriguing "machine-mechanics."  What I mean be "machine-mechanics" is that you can configure certain cards to build a "machine" that takes a raw material input (e.g., stone, wood, sand, etc.) and produce victory points, money, etc. as an output. If you want to play a whole game based purely on "machine-mechanics" try Infernal Contraption or Magic: The Gathering (especially the Mirrodin Set).  It is a somewhat light game that should be fun for families, however, the mechanics are advanced enough to deter some "nongamers."  Awards: Deutscher Spiele Preis 2007 (Germany); Vinner av Årets Familiespill 2007 (Norway); InQuest Gamer Top100-#66 (2007); Tric Tac de Bronze (Bronze) 2007; GAMES Magazine Game of the Year (2008); Hall of Games (Austria); Jda "Juego del Año en España" (Spain) 2008.

RATING: 7
Theme is based on buiding a cathedral in 12th century England. Using the mechanic of placing workers into areas (like Caylus), earn gold, get resources (sand, wood or stone) and convert resources into VPs.  The trick in this game is that everyone has 3 workers to place, but they are all put into a bag and pulled out randomly. The first workers out have to pay a higher cost in gold to be placed. There are many things to keep track of, and there is the tension of trying to get the right craftsman first or produce the right resources while balancing your money.  This is a decent game, and needs more plays to understand the nuances.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Pillars of the Earth is a beautiful game. Its cards and board are finely detailed, but not overly busy. Although superfluous, its turn counter is the best I've ever seen. The wooden components are colorful and easy to differentiate. The box insert, while thematic, unfortunately doesn't separate the game pieces very well.

The main problem with PotE is that the game rewards players who specialize in a single Resource more than those that try a more balanced approach. After a single game, most players will recognize this fact and specialize in every future play-through. Because there are only 3 basic Resources, this trend towards specialization will undermine any competition for Craftsmen or Resources, removing important tension from a 2-3 player game. In a 4-player game competition is restored, but likely only for 1 Resource. The 2 players competing for the same Resource will have lower scores than the 2 that have no competition. This single flaw cripples PotE as the result of each game can be predicted based on what players did in turn 1.

No change in PotE's superb game mechanics would have been necessary to solve this problem either. What is needed is to change a large percentage of the Craftsmen to require 2 or more different Resources for conversion into victory points.

Also, as only 4 new Craftsmen are available for purchase each day, adding 1 or 2 more Craftsmen to each daily pile would add the element of the unknown already present in the Events and Privileges decks, but lacking in the Craftsmen.

Ken Follet's novel Pillars Of The Earth described how simple design flaws can bring down an otherwise lovely cathedral and this game suffers the same fate. Just a little more testing could have made for a truly magnificent game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Pillars of the Earth is the quintessential worker placement game.  Every round people first take turns selecting resources and workers.  Immediately after this people take turns placing their action pawns on the actions they would like to do this turn, they have three pawns each.  

The tricky part is that turn order at this point is decided upon pulling the pawns out of a bag.  In the early part of the round if a pawn of yours is pulled you have to decide to place it for 7 gold, or pass for later.  Next will decide to place for 6, next is 5, and so on.

Players acquire points by converting goods, money, or combination of the two, into points.

This game is a joy to play and, having read the book, I appreciate all of the subtle attachments they pull from the book.  Can't wait to play again!

RATING: N/A
It's an abstract euro with double worker placement about getting cubes and trasnforming thenm into victory points. You really don't believe you're building a cathedral. In fact, the wooden cathedral pieces, the most beautiful components, are only used to mark the current turn. What a waste!! But the art of the game is gorgeous. The board is Michael Mentzel's at his best! 

The game is very well designed. All their mechanisms and elements interact like a perfect clockwork. Everything has been thought in order to create a good game.

Maybe it's a bit too simple for a 90-120 minutes game. You have tough decisions, that's true, but there's very little strategic and tactic content. And maybe it lacks some peculiar thing... A "soul", some would say. Maybe I'm not being totally fair, because it's a 2006 game, but nowadays there are games that do the same in less time.

Another think I didn't like is the big ammount of different card types and subtypes. The bureocracy is not very excessive, but it definitly lacks a bit of elegance in that sense.

It might not be a favourite, but it's still an excelent game I look forward to play again.

RATING: 6
Interesting...certainly something of Caylus in this, but it's a lot easier to co-ordinate your resources accurately, and - as if to compensate - a game much more open to close tactical play. Great component quality and artwork bely a swift game with plenty to be grasped in the initial couple of plays. The luck quotient is significant, but not as over-riding as you might be led to believe. A big letdown for me is the fact that the Master Builder locations are vastly unbalanced, and you really do need four players to get the most out of that phase of the game.

Further Thoughts: While this is a very fine game in many ways, there just doesn't seem to be the variety or the decision-making involved in many other worker-placement games. It's tighter, sure, but more fun? Really?

[i]First copy traded for Leaping Lemmings in October 2010 UK Maths Trade[/i]

RATING: 7
心得:
努力資源換分遊戲
特色是工人抽擺機制
規則:
*積木給每一回合的起始玩家，將24張鑄匠卡照背面數字分疊洗勻疊起，洗勻九張資源卡，特權卡除了最後一輪的兩張，剩下14張洗勻移除四張，疊在一起形成特權牌庫，事件卡洗勻移除四張放在事件格，補滿市場資源，放一個金屬在國王宮，四個灰色工人在城堡，黑色標記放在順位數字7上
*每個玩家拿一個顏色的所有工人，標記一個放在金錢軌20(照順位每後一位加一元)一個放在分數2，三個主建築師丟進袋中，拿三張起始鑄匠牌
*遊戲進行六輪，每輪三個階段，每輪開始前先開兩張特權卡跟兩張鑄匠卡放在對應位置，另外兩張鑄匠卡跟抽七張資源卡放在圖板下方
A 起始玩家開始，每個人輪流從下面九張挑牌走直到抽完或都pass(拿卡一定要夠付工人)，拿鑄匠要付錢，沒用到工人可領錢
*鑄匠卡上限五張，在拿就要選一張棄掉
B 主建築師擺放階段 起始玩家從袋中抽主建築師(第一抽可重抽)，抽到可付順位錢放，或是pass，不管有沒有放黑色順位標記都會移動，抽完最後一個後(一定0元)照順位數字由大到小免費放
C 工人都放完就解決行動格 1~13
1.開事件卡處理事件
2.有主建築師在此可規避此回事件 或是 從市場拿一個資源
3.工人拿錢
4.有主建築師拿特權卡，沒拿的棄掉
5.拿分
6-8.拿資源
9.擲骰付國王稅，有主建築師在這不用付，第一個在這的在拿一個金屬 (缺錢 每兩元扣一分)
10.拿鑄匠卡
11.拿兩個灰工人，下回合用完還回來
12.市場 照擺放順位輪流買賣一種貨物，賣是放到供應區，直到都pass則放回袋中(不能買之後賣同一種貨物)
13.每個人使用各自的鑄匠卡賺分，最後只能存五個資源
最後起始玩家放一個積木，如果有人搶起始則拿一個積木，否則順時鐘換人
*起始鑄匠，沒有石頭的不能賣石頭，沒有木頭不能買木頭，每有第三個不能用mason換分

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.6
A worker placement game, very similar to [b][thing=34635][/thing][/b], but with serious differences. Comparing it to [thing=34635][/thing]:
1) This game has a more significant "luck" factor, for me. Sure, [thing=34635][/thing] has dice rolling, but the dice rollings get evenly distributed one way or another. This game depends a lot on which master builder gets drawn first; and there's nothing you can to about it!
2) Much more fiddy. There is a lot of housekeeping to do between rounds, flip cards, replenish market... [thing=34635][/thing] plays much more straightforwardly.
3) Pillars has a much more straightforward scoring. [thing=34635][/thing] gives you different paths to victory and, thus, more options.

Last and most important: I cannot enjoy Pillars of the Earth as much as [thing=34635][/thing], which is a very fun game. I give Pillars its fair share of credit, it has very nice mechanisms, great components and outstanding outwork, but as long as [thing=34635][/thing] exists, the latter will supersede this game.

A fine game with beautiful artwork and a great theme. The first couple of plays, Pillars game was the perfect ten. It was elegant, full of theme and threw in a big chunk of luck that, in my opinion, spiced up the game-experience.  

But after some more plays I begun to see the weakness: replayability is only mediocre to poor. At the beginning there seemed to be so much to explore, but soon enough it felt like Pillars was playing it self. After some 15 plays it felt more like a normal 7 than the perfect 10. Still a fine game, but not exceptional by any standards.

I inconsequently rate it somewhat in-between my first and latest impressions. I did really love the first five or six plays, and that's got to be counted for something.

RATING: 6
Very, very nice to look at.  However, it often felt like I was getting played by the game.  There are only so many ways to get victory points, and you can almost work it out to a threshold of how much gold you are willing to spend per victory point.  If you are able to stick to your chosen ratio, and it's lower than everyone elses, you'll win.  There's an interesting mechanism of putting master builders out as they are drawn from the bag, but this can't really shut you down too extremely bad.  The event cards may be a little too random compared to the rest of the game.  I like how you need to be prepared for events, but with how open and obvious the rest of the game is, the complete and sometimes gamebreaking events can lead to some times where you just have to throw your hands up and take it like a man (or woman).  Note, I won the game I played, just didn't care too much for it as a repeat performance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Enjoyable and quite beautiful card-drafting and worker-placement game. The usual: compete for resources, combine and convert into victory points.

Better games in this genre exist. There's not much that keeps me coming back to this one, and it does have one or two things I don't like about it. Mainly, it's all about efficiency, but the order is which players place workers is random. So you can spend the time calculating what space is the most valuable for you, but it's quite possible that all the other players can place two or all three workers before you can place your first one. So much luck has no place in an efficiency-game like this. Second, it is quite long, which combined with the luck factor can be frustrating.

If you don't mind the randomness, though, this is an interesting game with nice interlocking elements.

Worker placement seems to be hit or miss for me, where the misses are the games that a) give you too much options to calculate your own most efficient path without much need to figure out what the other players need, and b) throw in randomness without much purpose. Like Stone Age, Pillars misses more than it hits.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This oldie had been on my Want to Play list for a long time.  Finally got to play it and it fell pretty flat.  It is Min/Max the Euro.  It's often compared to Stone Age, but in some ways it feels more like Power Grid.  Similar to Power Grid with improving power plants, you are building a hand of 5 cards that increase over the game in how efficiently they convert resources into points, but without the tension of Power Grid of only having 3 slots and having to auction them.  Your spending workers (kind of like a reusable $12) to get cards that give you cubes, and each round will vary in their availability, but the amount of resources you get per worker doesn't change over time like the market dynamics in Power Grid.  Different than Power Grid, much of the game centers around worker placement with this interesting dynamic that the order that you get to place your 3 workers depends on when they get pulled from a bag, with earlier pulled workers having a greater cost if you want to place them early or wait until all the workers have been pulled.  It's interesting, but because of this uncertainty the game lacks a lot of the tension in other worker placement games where you try to predict where each player will place their first worker and whether you can wait a round or need to go now, etc.  There's a 1st player spot, but that only matters for buying cards at the beginning and has not affect on the worker placement sequence.  This game just feels really dated and doesn't hold up to other worker placement games or even its heavier predecessor Caylus, which I think is still great.

RATING: 8
An excellent game.  Wonderful components and a beautiful board.  A lot of mechanics being played at once, but the game is easy to manage and learn and does not get overly complicated.  Plays quick with great strategy.  

Strategy - Use the early rounds to collect goods, especially metal, and money.  Pick up privilege cards that will help you the entire game and as many victory points in the Priory as possible.  Use the later rounds to score as many points as possible with the glassblower/bell maker and larger conversion workers.  Save as much gold as possible and use the Goldsmith in the last round for big points.

2007 Plays = 1
2008 Plays = 5

RATING: 9
This is my favourite "traditional board game" that I've played so far.
I love the breadth and depth of choices each player makes during all steps of the game. There is almost no down time for players as everybody is interested in the action at all times. Every choice is a difficult one with so many options of where to play your master builders or what to buy with your workers.
All this combined with a lovely theme from an entertaining books makes for a masterful game.
I highly recommend the expansion which fixes some issues with worker placement and makes the game fun with all the way up to 6 players.

RATING: 7.5
Played a two player game. Final score is 54-53. It is very well balance for 2 player. I think it is mainly because each player gets to put 3 master builders on the board in phase 2 and I can see this change when it is a 3 or 4 player game. In 3 or 4 player game, there is a chance you will put different number of master builder on the among players. I have mix feeling after my first play. I find the first few rounds of the game to be quite boring. I was doing nothing much other than keeping myself balanced, meaning not over spent and score enough points. However, the last two rounds are so satisfying because the much better craftsman cards become available. I scored big in my last round and won. However, I won't call this engine building though. In our game, we went with total different strategy. I was trying to keep myself balanced, my opponent was trying to score as many points as possible each round. I fell behind quite a bit going to the final round and I totally caught up because i had been collecting metal combine with extra income per metal after each round. However, my opponent almost won if he had not made one mistake in the final round. 

So I don't know. You can win without planning way ahead of time and there is really no need to build your "engine". There is also some luck element in the game when it gets to 3 or 4 player game. I find the game starting pretty slow. It turns out to be quite exciting in the final two rounds. I think the game is best with 3. Will play more and try the expansion.

The rules are very easy to follow and it can play in about 90 minutes with four experienced players. There's not quite enough resource competition in the two player game for my liking, but it's not too bad. Being start player in the last round is a must in a four player game - too much of a lucky advantage in this and who gets to place their master builder first in the final two rounds kind of spoils an otherwise entertaining game. 24 points each for two of the players (more than one third of their scores), and about half that for the other two players (because they weren't the start player or drawn early out of the bag ), is what you can expect out of this game. This distribution system verges on the annoying, certainly on the frustrating.

RATING: 4
Probably a good game for its time, but it has been surpassed. Stone Age is more elegant, and AoEIII more satisfying. The randomness of the Master Builder phase drives me absolutely nuts (especially since mine are always drawn after every spot I want has been taken). The game heavily rewards experience and knowing the cards. The game is also not very well-balanced, particularly with the gold-producing Carpenter (and especially in 2er games). The game often comes down to a crap-shoot in the last round of who is the start player, and whose Master Builder gets pulled out of the bag first.

Like it with 3. 4 players is way too crowded for the board (you're forced to take god-awful actions with your last worker), and 4 only makes the Master Builder phase even more random. It is playable with 4 if you use the placement rule from the expansion. 2 is unplayable without the expansion, due to the predictability of the cards, and the small but significant advantage the player who obtains the gold-producing Carpenter enjoys.

This game is made excellent with the expansion set.

RATING: 9
[3 plays] Although there is nothing here that makes me say, "Wow, I've never seen anything like that before!", I am always still grinning and enjoying the tension building as the game progresses. This is a very well produced and a very solid game. I am impressed and look forward to many more games of Pillars.

PotE is a resource and worker placement game that seems like a cross between AoEIII and Stone Age, despite being published well before either of them. Knowledge of the novel is not critical, but the theme is fun. Each player is trying to contribute victory points toward the building of the cathedral while competing for limited resources and craftsmen. It has an economic engine buildup, which I often enjoy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The game borrows from Caylus and it shows even down to the theme: getting resources to build a cathedral. Each turn, players use their builders and/or money to draft cards. These are then later exchanged for resources. Their master builders are then placed on a variety of different spots around the city, each with a different ability (exemption from tax, acquire event cards, marketplace to buy/sell goods). The ultimate goal being to process cubes or money through a variety of craftsmen in exchange for VPs.

Rinse and repeat for 6 rounds and determine winner. This means that some action rounds are rather processional and there wasn't a real sense of decision. Some clever intertwined mechanics works well together. The game has some meat on its bones and feels less forced than Caylus. This falls just outside the middleweight category but isn't a the heavyweight either! I think that's a flaw against it. All up, a good solid game but nothing new here.

[2011-07-30]
Még tavaly, több mint egy éve meghallgattam a szabályát, de végül nem futottam neki a játéknak, mert kicsit nehéznek éreztem az akkori társastudásomhoz. Most lassan 250 különböző játék ismerete után sem mondom könnyűnek a játékot. Nagyon sok mindenre kell figyelni ebben a munkáslehelyezős játékban, de cserében egy komoly, jó játékot kapunk kellemes játékélménnyel. A játékosok egy katedrálist építenek. 12 munkásukkal 3 fajta nyersanyagot szereznek, amit építőmestereik segítségével a piacon eladhatnak vagy mesterembereik segítségével győzelmi pontra váltanak. Ezen túl van még egy speciális nyersanyag, amit csak építőmesterrel lehet szerezni, illetve több hely különböző előnyök megszerzéséhez. A játékot nem játszani nehéz, hanem jól játszani nem könnyű. Sok helyre kell figyelni, a munkásokat, építőmestereket jól, megfelelő helyre lehelyezni, közben figyelni a mesterembereinkre is, akikre még különböző szabályok is vonatkoznak. A játék tartalmaz némi szöveget is kártyákon, ezért némi nyelvtudás sem árt hozzá, bár a fordítással tökéletesen játszható a játék. Az építőmesterek lehelyezési mechanizmusát először túl nagy szerencseszerepnek éreztem a játékban, de rájöttem jól kitalált dologról van szó. Így végülis nehéz bármilyen negatív dolgot mondanom. Sokan a Stone Age-hez hasonlítják, de annál jóval komolyabb darab és szerintem tökéletesen megfér egy gyűjteményben mindkét játék, mert a Stone Age könnyedebbsége több társaságban eladhatóvá teszi a szóban forgó játékhoz képest.

RATING: 8.5
Wygrane:

Ja - 1/1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5.5
Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting. Drafting, drafting, drafting.

If you like drafting this one's got it.  Drafting for me really hits and misses, but with PotE the card draws are timed so that they are totally random.  It pacing of the deck is finely balanced, so it works for me.  What is totally screwy is the draws for the turn order.  In some regards I like this particular part of the system, but bad luck on the draw can really hump someone hard.  Right where you wipe.  It isn't far fetched to say that the draws can cause a winning player to lose the game.  I guess it could be worse, it could be all auctions.

What I don't like is that it requires players to have a front loaded understanding of the values of cards.  On first play, I don't think it is clear to players that some cards are just better than others. Many of the same card faces just upgrade in value at the same price. I am a little concerned about the Monk that gives you a bonus to all VPs in the Monastery, it seems a little screwy. Because of the way it is timed, early cards are worth less, so the game escalates. This is fine, as it forces you to mix it up and continue to build yourself up, but the large return at the end of the game makes the first couple turns useless, it's not like you had to do anything at the beginning to get anything at the end, you just have to Keep Up with the Joneses.  This system may also turn off a newbie when they spend too much for a quickly outdated card.   I appreciate how tight the game is, as it is a neck and neck race right to the bitter end.  Money is tight and resources are tighter.  I like the penalties from firing the low men on the totem pole and all the decisions that come with it.  All in all a fine enough well crafted Euro for those that want that sort of thing.  

I don't think I'd turn down a game, but frankly it is just so close to Caylus in so many ways that it is clear to see how it is an inferior product for what I want out of a game. I'd probably rather play Caylus or Leonardo DaVinci, but Pillars is shorter and more accessible, so this fulfills a purpose.  I could play this with people that aren't used to really heavy Euros (or for those that like indirect passive aggression rather than direct conflict in games) and still get a kick out of it.

RATING: 8
This game is a winner and current leader of the pack for best of 2007. Its a typical Euro with resource management and role selection being the main mechanics. But it mashes in some other elements as well. There is a wonderful catch up mechanism for cash (workers in the mill), the economics of whether to spend for placement or wait for free actions, building upon a set strategy and activating resources a la Power Grid. The boards is gorgeous to look at and the round marker (building an actual wooden cathedral) is unique and thoughtful. Overall, the game oozes theme, is easy to teach and plays in about 2 hours.

RATING: 6
It's a resource game - you collect cubes, then trade them in for VP (or money). Like Caylus but shorter, simpler, and less repetitive. Maybe a little too much randomness for me (the order the cards come up and the order the builder tokens are pulled from the bag). Also, there's not enough choice of what to do, especially in a 4-player game: even with only 3 workers eah, those last few workers often have nowhere interesting to go -- and they were drawn in random order!

5.5/10 for the game with original rules. However, it's easy to incorporate the expansion rule to bump it back to a 6/10: set aside 1 master builder per player. When a player places a master builder on the board for the first time, place the set-aside meeple of their color in a line. After players have placed their 2 meeples from the bag onto action spaces, in reverse order (last in first out) these third meeples get placed onto action spaces.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
It's a resource game - you collect cubes, then trade them in for VP (or money). In some ways it's like Caylus but shorter, simpler, and less repetitive version. 

I'm not actually sure how I feel about it. It plays pretty quickly, and it was fun ... but I almost feel like I have a greater appreciation for Caylus (and I'm not one of those Caylus fanboys)! There just seemed to be a bit too much randomness here (the way the cards come up and the order the builder tokens are pulled from the bag), and not enough choice of what to do (only 3 workers, drawn in random order).

10/3: the builder-pull is just too annoyingly random/frustrating ... dropping rating 0.5 to 5.5 for the game with original rules. However, it's easy to incorporate the expansion rule to make it a bit better: set aside 1 master builder per player. When a player places a master builder on the board for the first time, place the set aside meeple in line directly in front of the last meeple in that line. After all players have placed their 2 drawn meeples on the board, they place their third meeple as well -- according to the order of the line (reverse the order they were placed there).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Playing this game for the first time 8 years after it was released is probably a bit unfair to it since every other game that comes out nowadays is worker placement and everyone's trying to come up with something different to make their worker placement game the diamond in the rough.  And as a HUGE worker placement game fan AND critic I've played a lot of them and have seen some diamonds but also a lot of rough.

This is a fine game, it's well designed, has interesting decisions, and also teaches and plays easily and quickly.  But it is just kind of bland next to all the worker placement stuff available nowadays.

Each turn players start by either buying craftsman or supplies of resources.  Then when that phase is finished the starting player draws the players pawns randomly from a bag one at a time.  When a player's piece is drawn he can decide to pay the current amount to place his pawn or place it for free after all other pawns have been placed.  This is my favorite part of the game because it is a somewhat interesting and well designed approach to worker placement.  The first seven pawns placed in a turn (each player has 3) cost 7-1 gold to place.  This puts a cost on selecting highly sought after spaces while also giving you the option to take the "leftovers" for free.  The starting player also still gets an advantage since he is able to once that round put a piece back and reselect.

However aside from a very small selection of special ability cards that's all that gives this game any kind of personality.  The rest of the game is very standard worker placement actions leading up to having your craftsman just turn resources into points to represent building a cathedral (there's even 6 wooden pieces of the cathedral...which I guess is a nice aesthetic touch except they are just plain wood and I don't have the time or skill to paint them).

Very blah despite being well designed.  Perhaps in 2006 it was good but it certainly doesn't hold up in 2014.

RATING: 8
Very fine game overall.  Three things prevent me from rating it higher: (1) the luck in drawing pieces from the bag can be severe (it usually balances out, but sometimes it's brutal). (2) I wish there were extra characters for each turn, so you never know which ones would be in a given game.  (The characters have turn numbers on the back: 1, 2, etc.  All four for each turn are always available, though you don't know if you have to pay for them or can use your master builder to get them.) (3) We've noticed a "fall-behind loser" syndrome.  It's not really a runaway leader, but it has a similar effect.  That is, we've NEVER (in roughly ten games) had a game where the person in last place after turn 3 won the game.  But that may change as we learn the game more.  Otherwise, very nice time length for the type of game it is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I like worker placement games but I don’t play a lot of them and I almost never get exited about them, That is probably why I haven’t played this until now.  This game has a little twist to it by having two separate worker placement phases.  There is a phase where you actually place workers which I found slightly humorous and a phase where you place master builders.  Both phases have massively different game effects and work with a different mechanic.  The worker placement phase is a simple clockwise around the table affair of assigning workers to the available resource cards or claiming a new artisan.  During the master builder phase each player has their three master builders placed in a bag for a random draw.  The master builders are placed on the board where each spot on the board has an effect.  These affects can be event cards, artisan cards, tax collector, etc.  The thing it the player order here is massively important as there are a couple really good spots, some ok spots and some not so good spots.  This is mitigated by the fact that when your Builder is drawn from the bag you can pass or play the builder for a cost.  The cost ranges form 7 down to 0.  The first builder drawn costs 7 and then the cost goes down by one for each builder drawn.  This has an interesting effect where you can get really good stuff or you can pass and save you bling for future turns.  

I liked it and would readliy play it again.

RATING: 7.5
This game surprised me.  Now, I normally steer well clear of the very silly and mostly imaginary Euro/Ameritrash divide, however anyone taking the time to study my game ratings would realize that the only true, Euro-style games I really enjoy are those that aren't overly complex and don't take too long.  To be fair, this is certainly not an overly complex game, per se, once you've got a game under your belt (even though it does require a fair bit of thought, advance planning, and mapping out of the multiple paths to victory).  And the theme is, well, as good as can be expected from a Euro-style game, although the game play reminds me in almost no way of the book upon which the game is based (which is a very good thing, because I think the book is HIGHLY over-rated, and VERY poorly written).  So where was I going with this mini review?  Oh yes, all that to say that I've found a fairly involved, relatively long, thinly-themed, Euro-style game that I rather enjoy.  Mind you, my only experience is with 4 players, so I don't know how well it would scale with fewer players and therefore less competition.  And I'm also curious about this "Stone Age" game that has drawn so many comparisons.  Game design: 4/5  Personal enjoyment: 3.5/5  Total: 7.5/10

RATING: 7
[Based on 3 plays] This games gets a lot of comparison to Caylus. I see the comparison but really there is a lot of differences between the two games, including overall feel and gameplay. I don't think it quite lives up to Caylus.  Two things in particular stuck out to me.  I disliked the events that might be good or bad and were hard to plan for.  I feel in Caylus the difference between just losing or just winning is a good move here or there, in Pillars of the Earth it feels like it was a random event or the order the masters get pulled from the bag that makes just enough difference for who wins or loses.  An okay game but just not as satisfying to win or lose based on an event or master builder pull.  The game has grown on me some, I will grant it is more accessible and easier to play than Caylus.  The expansion helps mitagate from the master builder pull luck as well.

RATING: 5
Played once, then sold. Disappointing. I had read the book, and looked forward to something rich in theme about cathedral building. The cathedral pieces included are just turn markers - a waste of material. You try to maximize turning cubes into points without spending too much money. When that dry formula is the basis for a game, I think it needs to add some fun through opportunities for player interaction or for building a larger strategy than just what's best to do per move. This game lacks those two elements. I wouldn't go as far as saying it's badly designed or imbalanced or too luck-based (I wasn't bothered as much as others by the master builder draw). I can see why some people would like this a lot, but it's not for me.

RATING: N/A
Inghilterra inizio del XII secolo

A Kingsbridge sta per essere costruita la più grande e più bella cattedrale del paese. Partecipi a questa imponente opera e per ottenere la gloria che meriti dovrai gestire al meglio i capomastri e i lavoranti e procurarti le risorse necessarie al lavoro dei tuoi artigiani. Come intendi procedere? Chiederai udienza al Re per ottenere i metalli necessari? Cercherai di assicurarti il supporto di altre personalità influenti? Anche un soggiorno nel priorato può esserti d’aiuto, come un buon aggancio nella gilda degli artigiani…

Goditi una partita in pieno Medio Evo, come lo ha descritto Ken Follett nell’omonimo romanzo.

Elenco elementi di gioco:
1 Mappa da gioco
1 Cattedrale composta di sei parti
12 Capomastri
75 Carte
37 Lavoranti
9 Dischi segnapunti
82 Cubi “risorsa”
1 Dado, 1 Sacchetto
1 Libretto delle regole

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pillars is a very good production style game. Some have called it "Caylus Light" which isn't that far off.  It uses a similar "everyone choose your actions and then execute them in a specific order" mechanic.  It's certainly more forgiving and it seems to be pretty well balanced.  It does not seem to be nearly as deep as Caylus though.  Things will tend to unfold in much the same way each game.  What variety there is mainly comes from having other players disrupt your strategy.  The game plays well with two but may be a little better with three due to the increased competition for favorable actions.  With four the game becomes almost too dependent upon luck but it's still enjoyable. The biggest advantage over Caylus is the shorter length.  This game can easily be completed in an hour with experienced players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Knowing the rhythms of the 6 turns puts you at a huge advantage over players less familiar with what's coming up. Possibly falls between two stools: too long for some; too much luck for others. One particular thing that's starting to irritate me: each turn, 4 craftsman become available; two are purchased, two gained through placement. You can use turn order to claim first choice on purchasing, but sod's law decrees that when you do that, you won't be able to buy the craftsman you want, and you won't get lucky when master builders are drawn out of the bag.

RATING: 7
I've only played the 6 player expansion with The Pillars of the Earth, but it was a very good (if complex) game for 6 people. It kept the tense and tight feel that other games have with fewer people, and had enough random elements to make the game interesting and unpredictable. It is a little difficult to play for the first time because of the long set of rules (it is like starting with a full town built in Caylus, it is hard to decide where to go at first), but I think future playings will make the game shine as a good 6 player choice.

Update: The game seems best with 5. And although I do like the game, I absolutely hate the random drawing for turn order of the Master Builders. It is way too random, especially when the game is only 6 turns long and it can really influence what you get over someone else. I feel like that single mechanic needs to be redone, but everything else fits perfectly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Worker-placement game based on Ken Follett's historical novels. The aim is to acquire victory points through a combination of craftsmen and resources as you play your part in the construction of a 12th Century cathedral. The strategic challenge, like in all good games of this genre, is to optimise the amount of points you can obtain with the craftsmen you have at your disposal whilst also giving consideration to not reducing your finances too much. The mechanic is very well-balanced and reduces the likelihood of a runaway leader and there is just the right level of randomness provided by the event and privilege cards. The gameplay also ties in very well with the theme, which is a feature that can often be absent in this type of game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Worker placement with two types of workers.  Gather resources and turn them into money and points.  The dutch auction for actions on the second type of workers is pretty neat and adds a bit of randomness to the game.  Plays quickly and the enjoyment to length ratio is high. 2009/05/07

RATING: 8
Very fun worker-placement game centered around an interesting theme (cathedral building) from an excellent book by the same name.  I always feel like I'm doing well, even when I lose, and I always want to play again to improve my score.  Games are also usually very close at the end.

The biggest problem I have is the lack of variety.  There are not enough craftsman cards.  Four are played each round, but there are only four cards per round in the box.  Thus, the same four are always available.  An expansion could really help this game.

RATING: 7
This game was quite enjoyable, the board is very nice looking but appears exceeding daunting at first glance.  The play however was a cleaver ballencing act of money, cards, bids, location etc.  Everything played really well together.  The mechanism of drawing master builders from the bag added a brilliant balance of light luck with cleaver strategic choice.  Do you spend the 7 gold and go first, or do you wait and be close to last.  I love the cathedral pieces but I do think starting the game with one as a turn counter is somewhat silly as we are supposed to be building it, cute however.  I wish some of the knowledge of the cards was available to new players, events, stuff like the bell master last turn ETC make the game a slight unfair advantage to new people, could use some variant cards to mix it up a little.  All and all good production quality, great looking game, interesting play.

RATING: 9
An awful lot to like in this gamer's game, although I think it fails in one major regard - it borrows heavily from Caylus but, other than being a bit shorter, doesn't improve on the mechanics much. I'm also not sure I like the huge luck element of drawing placement order randomly. While paying to place first should even things out, it doesn't always seem to. The sweet spot seems to be getting lucky enough to have your pieces drawn in middle position, where you're placing early and not paying too much to do it. That said, this is a very promising game and I expect it will break into my play rotation on a regular basis.

Good game! As of now, I can merely say that this has quite a lot in common with Maestro Leonardo and the other way around. You send off your workers to get you ressources or have them work in the wool manufacture to gain you money. For your "builders" there are several options on the board to choose from, but you only have three builders. And money gets pretty scarce sometimes, so each player probably will have to pass on a leader at least once. That is, where draw of luck has a little too much impact, because the later your "pawn" is drawn (from the bag), the less you have to pay for an action. That is, if the others choose to take an action. Prices vary from 7 gold to 0 gold per action. With each action taken, the price drops down by 1. I think, to even it out a scale of 5,5,4,4,3,3,2,2 instead of 7,6,5,4,3,2,1,0 (not quite sure about the numbers) would fix really bad luck of draw. Which happened to one of our players not only once but three turns in a row (ok, really, really bad luck) His builder got drawn at 7 and his next at 6, so his two actions cost him 13,  whereas the ones later on could take their actions almost for free (consider 4 players, 3 builders each) Other than that, this is a very nice game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This was the first worker placement game that I either played or owned.  When Agricola came out, and proved to be a much deeper and richer experience, I traded away Pillars without a thought.  

And, as it goes every now and then, I realized that it was a pretty good game.  While it is not as dynamic or varied as Stone Age, nor is it as rewarding or thematic as Agricola, it does stand on its own. 

I figure that if you have played any of the worker placement games, or if you have played a bunch of them, Pillars is still worth a look.  The placement of the master builders is a really slick and tense mechanic that is much more than a gimmick.  

It could be argued that Pillars is rote, unoriginal, and uninspired.  But I would argue that the same could be said of nearly any game.

RATING: 8
Beautiful looking board & components, except the small size cards which I generally hate (although at least this game doesn't involve shuffling large stacks of them).  Very tight economic system that forces difficult choices.  Basic strategy is simple -- get good craftsmen, get resources, and turn that into lots of VPs.  But the difference between winning and losing any game is subtle -- lots of little decisions that add up to a couple of VPs.

I think this game is great with 3 and very good with 4.  I'm not thrilled with it for 2 because there are too many good spots on the board so the builder placement decisions (and therefore cash management to preserve cash to place expensive builders) aren't as painful.

RATING: 5
I know everyone originally called this "Caylus Lite" but then stopped when Caylus Magna Carta came out, since that was probably "Caylus Lite" instead. However, the master builders really ruined this game for me, which is unfortunate since Pillars had a lot of things going for it, those things being the features that it borrows from Caylus (i.e., building an engine to turn resource cubes into victory points).  The random draw of the master builders from the bags (along with the randomness of the cards) takes the concepts of Caylus out of a game that involves no luck and puts them into a game that involves a lot of luck.  This may be just what others were hoping for and enjoy most, but it's definitely not something I personally enjoy at all, especially given the alternative game (i.e., Caylus) that could be played instead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The basic game is fairly straightforward. You get resources. You convert resources to VP's with craftsmen. You can get better craftsmen that more efficiently convert resources to VP's. That's essentially it.

Each round there are two phases. In the first phase you're drafting from a common pool of resources and craftsmen. In the second phase you're placing your workers on actions spots. The interesting twist to the game is that workers are drawn from a bag randomly. The first worker drawn has to pay 7 coins to take an action or pass and go stand in line. Each subsequent worker has to pay one coin less until it reaches zero. after the bag empties, the guys in line get to deploy. So you might get drawn early and really want to go first and pay the 7 coins, although money can be tight, so you won't be doing that often.

As the game progresses the craftsmen get better and better at converting goods (but you can only keep 5 resources from one round to the next). This was true to the point where I got the majority of my score in the last two rounds of the game.

I enjoyed the game. But... there's a fair bit of randomness in worker bag selection mechanism, as you really want to be chosen early, but not too early. If you're drawn too soon or too late, you'll just get screwed on worker placement. On one hand, this is the most interesting thing in the game, as it makes for some interesting choices. On the other hand, this is the most problematic issue in the game, as you can just get screwed. And the randomness seems fairly significant given the length/depth of the game. There's also a random event every round that might pile on to the randomness. I'm not sure if it's quite "too much" randomness or not.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
I like drafting cards and I like the Master Builder lottery (in which in random order players are offered the chance to draft whatever they want, at a price that starts high but continually decreases) but in this game there seems to be no differentiation or urgency to anything you're drafting! The three main cube resources do exactly the same thing, just with varying parameters and synergies. As another example there are four "pay no taxes" worker placement spots on the board, in a game that maxes out at four players.

The decisions just feel inconsequential. Clearly they're not, as they (plus some luck) determine the winner. But it's so loose and unengaging.

World Without End is much more interesting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
At the beginning of the 13th century, construction of the greatest and most beautiful cathedral in England begins. Players are builders who try to contribute the most to this cathedral's construction and, in so doing, score the most victory points. Gameplay roughly consists of using workers to produce raw materials, and then using craftsmen to convert the materials into victory points. Workers may also be used to produce gold, the currency of the game. Players are also given three master builders each turn, each of which can do a variety of tasks, including recruiting more workers, buying or selling goods, or just obtaining victory points. Getting early choices with a master builder costs gold, as does purchasing better craftsmen. Players must strike a balance between earning gold to fund their purchases and earning victory points.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

UPDATE: Played again recently, and the game has lost some of its appeal for me. Even with the new rules for drawing builders from the bag, that part feels too luck driven. Consequently lowered my rating with one point. I guess better games have surpassed it in recent years.

RATING: 4
Well, I was predisposed not to like this game because I didn't like and couldn't finish the book, so that might explain why I didn't like it. I found it overly random and dull. The very little in this game that is new and original (the master builder drawing) is just stupid, while the majority is borrowed from other better and funner games. I don't usually enjoy drafting actions even in the best of circumstances, when there are other interesting things going on in a game, but in Pillars, the other part of the game is more drafting! Bleh. 

On the other hand, I really liked that it was so short.
