=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Excellent components, pretty board, some interesting mechanics-  and virtually inescapable parity.

all the actions are so close in eventual point value, and there is little to no time constraint for accomplishing particular things or reason to worry about availability of resources-  even the good point converters are in adequate abundance.  you're not really "building" an economic engine, so much as processing what you get.

i disagree with the luck complaint-  there is certainly luck involved, but the luck impact is minimal- the problem is that unless you specifically mess up (like repeatedly generating more resources than you can hold/use, or spending gold down so you lose points from taxation), you will be a contender.

high parity.  no relevant blocking. essentially no time constraint on resource processing.  no opportunism or long-term strategizing that will play out in an adequate way.  almost nothing to allow intelligent decisions to be the deciding factor.

RATING: 5.5
Worker-placement game that feels too much like an unfocused patchwork which dragged on a little in our first play. Not a fan of the random events and felt there was too much luck in the card draw. The luck makes the game more tactical in nature but the choices available generally feel uninteresting. There are several other worker-placement games that are either more interesting mechanistically, more focused or integrate theme better that I would rather play, but I would oblige to try again if there was insistence. But bottom line is too much luck and too dry for game length. Components and art is however top-notch and manages to invoke the source material to some extent.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This game is a lot like Stone Age, in my opinion.  The event cards in the game are a bit too extreme, in my opinion.  You can put a lot of planning into a turn, and then have it blown away by an event card that throws a huge random swing, to de-balance the game.

I still like the game, and I think there are ways to counteract this.  Next time I play, I'm going to turn up three event cards at a time (in advance), and then when it is time to draw an event card, I'll roll a die to pick one of the three.

What I like about the game is the wide range of choices each turn.  The results of the choices are extremely different, yet seem to be very well balanced.  Like 3-4 player stone age, there is a little opportunity to play defensively by blocking other player's moves, but there are usually alternative choices that are nearly as good, so defensive play is not a necessity to win.  

I'll bet 2-player is more cutthroat, but I don't have any experience there yet.

RATING: 6
Lavish.  I love the art style, especially the board.  I've only played once, but from what I can tell, the gameplay is solid.  There are a bunch of neat mechanics, and the choices often are difficult.  The production and building, with requisite economy and victory points systems draws obvious inspiration from other classics, but the systems are different enough to make Pillars worth a try, if not a buy.  

I'm a bit concerned that this game is a little too random to hold the long-term attention of serious gamers, but too intricate to really appeal to a lighter crew.  The ability to grab an early powerful privilege card is fairly random, and may be enough to swing the game all on its own.  I'm not sure that seven gold is enough to pay for the master builder actions at the start of the game.  But that's based off of only one play, so don't roast me if I'm eventually proven wrong.  If given the choice to play this again, I'll probably suggest [gameid=22545].

RATING: 5
Wow.  There's a lot going on in this game, and your choices are going to be limited almost every turn.  There's no small amount of randomness, given the event cards, and the way that the advantage and craftsmen cards come out, but the drawing for turn order out of the bag is probably the most significant part of the game governed by luck.  It can be frustrating when you need a specific card, and are too far down in the turn order to take advantage of it, though.  Still, the game has a lot of characteristics I like, and there are always some extra rules to help alleviate some of that luck.  Very interested in playing again.  I just wonder how long it will take before the game "plays out," what with the limited differences in the different plays.

2 plays: 4 players

RATING: N/A
At the beginning of the 13th century, construction of the greatest and most beautiful cathedral in England begins. Players are builders who try to contribute the most to this cathedral's construction and, in so doing, score the most victory points. Gameplay roughly consists of using workers to produce raw materials, and then using craftsmen to convert the materials into victory points. Workers may also be used to produce gold, the currency of the game. Players are also given three master builders each turn, each of which can do a variety of tasks, including recruiting more workers, buying or selling goods, or just obtaining victory points. Getting early choices with a master builder costs gold, as does purchasing better craftsmen. Players must strike a balance between earning gold to fund their purchases and earning victory points.

Unfortunately all these good aspects are ruined by a very high luck factor. Events are too random and too powerful and only one player each turn can decide to protect himself , it would be better to have more spaces like for taxes exemption.
Your worker can all come out very soon or very late ruining your plans completely and the additional rule of keeping one of them aside and have them placed in reverse order is not enough. There is TOO MUCH LUCK for such a highly strategic game.

RATING: N/A
Grail game. Literature based medieval worker placement to be the most successful contributor in the construction of Kingsbridge Cathedral.  Elegant play with two main phases. Begins with a card draft of craftsmen and resources followed by an interesting pouch draw for "pay or pass"  worker action placement phase.  Interesting value decisions on size of work force employed to complete actions.  Tight gold with plenty of ways to foil opponent plans. Satisfying progression of developing your team of craftsmen to leverage resource conversion, work force and gold generation in a competitive challenge that integrated well with its theme.

RATING: 8
This is a good one.  It's worker placement like Agricola, but with an added randomness twist, because the turn order is determined by pulling the pieces out of a bag.  More decisions come into play when your piece is pulled early in the turn, because the first 7 placements cost money.  Another nice aspect is that the more powerful craftsmen appear later in the game, and grabbing these can propel lagging players into contention or even the lead.

RATING: 3
{ P - [3] 4 5 }

Post-Caylus worker placement with less complexity and more uncertainty. After a couple games discerning players should experience instances where they feel very powerless. The process of placing workers is one example: players don't place one worker at a time, they're simply pulled from a bag so chance can determine whether you get the opportunity to place on a desired spot for the desired cost. I like the idea - an option to pay or bid to place a worker before others but deciding randomly who can is not the right execution. Other issues: only seven of the nine commodity cards see play during any given round, so there's the chance resources some players need can become arbitrarily scarce and force them to waste a master builder at the market. Event cards are another blind-fate mechanic that randomly rewards or rebukes players, and not going last is so vital to have a shot at new craftsmen during the resource buying phase that turn order must switch every turn. This process isn't automatic; you must use a master builder. The original first player will only need to spend once while the other two players spend twice.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A
Gruitpoort Spelavond

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Pillars of the Earth is a simple yet fun worker placement , resource conversion game. It scales well with 2-4 players but different strategies present themselves based on the number of people. While one strategy might seem like a guaranteed victory one game, the next game it might be irrelevant. It also makes you think about taking  some risks while never really keeping you that far away from potential victory. The game also plays easily within an hour to hour and a half once you understand the mechanics of the game.

I have played this game more times than is shown but that was well before I joined BGG. Having played a multitude of other games since rating this originally, I could see how this rating might actually rise a bit as it is quite a great game when taken in to context of the complexity, etc. It also has some interesting game mechanics that I haven't seen in other games, like the pulling the pawns from the bag to determine who places first and the associated cost to do so.

I could see how the game could feel "samey" over time as there are limited cards in the game and you generally can figure out strategies from the get-go. The variability comes from the timing of when those cards come out, how contested the particular cards are and whether or not you've left a little wiggle-room in your strategy to adapt and/or money in your coffers to over pay to place first for that card that you really want.

All in all I think that this is a very enjoyable "gateway +" game and I'd like to find the expansion to it as I think that it would bump up the rating of this game from very good to great.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game falls in the same category as games like Caylus and Keythedral, with which it shares some similarities, namely the kind of game where you have to optimize converting your resources, in this case money and workers (which also translate directly into money) to cubes and then turning those cubes into either more resources, or VPs. This is not the kind of game that I typically enjoy, but as far as those games go, I thought this one was particularly well done. Sometimes a bit dry and mathematical, and the improving quality of the available worker cards as rounds go by feels a bit artificial, but the mechanics serve their purpose. Various event cards add a bit of extra theme and spice to the game. I liked the random drawing of the "Baumeisters" mechanic, to introduce a bit of uncertainty and excitement in the game. Then again, this same mechanic may determine the winner in the last rounds, as scores are close and the cards up for grabs in the last round will make all the difference. I do think there are enough ways to prepare for this final round and you can play around the luck factor. Not a Spiel des Jahres candidate, it is a tad too gamery for that, but all in all a very solid offering from Kosmos. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Never read the book. Love the game! Great worker placement
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
So I was totally wrong about this game.  I will admit that I scoffed at it when everyone was playing Pillars of the Earth a couple of months ago.  It did not interest me in the least at that time, and as it started to win awards, I often wondered to myself "what is the big deal?"

Well, it seems that I have been missing out.  Pillars of the Earth turned out to be quite a clever game in managing and converting resources into points.  I like the many choices the players can select from, as they assess what they can afford to give up and to take a chance at against the things they really need.  However, the other side of that is as the board starts to fill up, almost always someone will be left with options they don't need, and to me that indicates poor design when you are forced to do take an action that has absolutely no effects on you.  Tied to that is the drawing of the master builders.  It's true that those who get to select first will have to pay the most as well, but it is still possible for everyone else to go before you costing them virtually nothing if your color comes out last.  The fact there is no way to mitigate this chance, no matter how small, really hurts the game.

RATING: 7
After 2+ Plays: Despite the ubiquitous comparison to Caylus, I found this to be quite a different, though enjoyable, experience.  The luck didn't really bother me; I thought it was just right for a game of this length.  It doesn't have the depth of Caylus and I don't see it becoming a mainstay, but at this point I'm looking forward to another play.  Worth playing at least once to marvel at the beautiful board design: great art with 3 different number tracks seemlessly integrated.

RATING: 9
Is it Caylus light? I don't care it sure is a very nice game. Altough you find a lot of elements/mechanism elsewhere there's enough to justify this great game. Should be great as a nice game that fills the gap between medium and heavy game. Some luck but lots of decision to be made (is that correct english?). Overall an excellent game. Nice components, great dark graphics, good theme and well working mechanisms! Updated to a 9 after several plays with different groups.
